Dhihar to Multiple Wives Requires Multiple Expiation
Ruling on dhihar directed to multiple wives in one utterance

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on dhihar to multiple wives and explain the distinction from ila' in the number of expiations required.

Core Idea
Dhihar to each wife is a separate prohibited analogy; each requires its own expiation — unlike ila' where the oath is one.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya clarifies the ruling when a husband addresses all his wives with a single dhihar statement. The Hanafi school holds that multiple wives create multiple dhihars, each requiring a separate expiation. The author also distinguishes this from ila' (a vow to abstain), where only one expiation is needed even for multiple wives. Understanding the reasoning behind this distinction is key to applying the rule correctly.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the husband doing? He says 'You are all like my mother's back' to multiple wives in one utterance. The ruling: this constitutes multiple dhihars, one for each wife.
2
Determine the consequence
For each dhihar, an expiation (kaffarah) is wajib. He cannot lawfully approach any wife until all expiations are performed.
The text explicitly says 'ولا يحل وطؤهن حتى يؤدي الكفارات'
3
Compare with ila'
If he made ila' from all wives with a single oath, only one expiation is required. Why the difference?
4
Analyze the talil (cause)
Ila' expiation is for violating the name of Allah (singular cause). Dhihar expiation is for removing the marital prohibition, which is multiple because each wife has her own prohibition.
The text says: 'وجه الفرق ... إنّ الكفّارة في الإيلاء لهتك حرمة اسم الله العظيم وهو غير متعدّد، وفي الظهار لرفع الحرمة وهي متعدّدة بتعدد المحل'
5
Note the exception for repetition
If dhihar is repeated to the same wife multiple times, expiations multiply unless the later statements are intended as emphasis, in which case they count as one.
The text says: 'إلا إن نوى بما بعد الأوّل تأكيداً فيصدق قضاء'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : وبأنتن ... الخ ؛ يعني إذا خاطب نساءه بكلمة واحدة وقال خطاباً إليهن : أنتن علي كظهر أمّي ونحو ذلك، يكون ذلك ظهاراً متعدّداً بحسب تعدد المخاطب، وتجب لكلّ ظهار كفّارة، ولا يحلّ وطؤهن حتى يؤدي الكفارات، وهذا بخلاف الإيلاء، فإنّه لو آلى منهنّ بكلمة واحدة وقال : والله لا أقربكن أربعة أشهر تلزمه كفارة واحدة. وجه الفرق على ما في «البحر» (۳) وغيره : إنّ الكفّارة في الإيلاء لهتك حرمة اسم الله العظيم وهو غير متعدّد، وفي الظهار لرفع الحرمة وهي متعدّدة بتعدد المحل ؛ ولهذا لو كرّر الظهار من امرأةٍ واحدةٍ مرتين أو أكثر في مجلس أو مجالس تتكرر الكفارة بتعدده إلا إن نوى بما بعد الأوّل تأكيداً فيصدق قضاء. كذا في «الفتح»(۳).
His saying: 'And (if he says) "You are..." etc.' means: If he addresses his wives with a single word and says addressing them: 'You are to me like the back of my mother' and the like, this constitutes multiple dhihars according to the number of addressees, and for each dhihar an expiation is obligatory, and it is not lawful for him to have intercourse with them until he performs the expiations. This is contrary to ila', for if he made ila' from them with a single word and said: 'By Allah, I will not approach you for four months', only one expiation is binding on him. The basis of the difference, according to al-Bahr and others, is that the expiation in ila' is for violating the sanctity of the great name of Allah, which is not multiple, while in dhihar it is for removing the prohibition, which is multiple due to the multiplicity of the subject. For this reason, if he repeats dhihar from a single woman twice or more in one session or multiple sessions, the expiation is repeated with its repetition, unless he intends by the subsequent (statements) an emphasis, then his statement is believed (in court). As in al-Fath.
Source pages p. 415

Commentary
This passage clarifies a ruling on dhihar when pronounced to multiple wives in one statement. The commentator explains that each wife is individually addressed, so the statement creates multiple dhihars, each requiring its own expiation. He cannot lawfully approach any of them until all expiations are performed. The commentator then distinguishes this from ila' (a vow to abstain from intercourse), where only one expiation is needed even if addressed to multiple wives. The difference lies in the cause of expiation: in ila', it is for profaning the name of Allah (by breaking an oath), which is one; in dhihar, it is for removing the marital prohibition (by the forbidden analogy), which is multiple as the prohibition pertains to each wife individually. The passage also notes that if a man repeats dhihar to the same wife multiple times, expiations multiply unless he intends the later statements as emphasis, in which case his intention is accepted judicially.

Key Terms
ظهار (dhihar): A husband's statement comparing his wife to a female relative forbidden for marriage, e.g., 'You are like my mother's back', which makes marital relations prohibited until expiation is performed.
كفّارة (kaffarah): Expiation, an act of worship required to amend a sin or prohibited act. For dhihar, options include freeing a slave, fasting two consecutive months, or feeding sixty poor people.
إيلاء (īlā’): A vow to abstain from intercourse with one's wife for more than four months.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a man says to his three wives collectively: 'You are all to me like my mother's back', how many expiations are required according to the Hanafi school?
A) One
B) Three
C) None
D) Only for the first wife mentioned

Key Takeaway
Dhihar to multiple wives in one utterance creates multiple expiations, one for each wife, because each wife's prohibition is separate.

The Expiation for Dhihar: Freeing a Slave
Ruling on the expiation for dhihar and conditions of the slave

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on freeing a non-Muslim slave for dhihar expiation and explain the usul principle of 'haml al-mutlaq ala al-fard al-kamil'.

Core Idea
The Quranic 'raqabah' is absolute: any sound slave suffices, regardless of faith or minor defects.

In this passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah, the commentator explains the ruling on the expiation for dhihar. The matn states that freeing a slave fulfills the expiation, and it is permissible to free either a Muslim or a non-Muslim slave. The commentator then clarifies the condition of soundness, applying the Hanafi usul principle that an absolute command is interpreted as the perfect individual.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its source
The matn says 'freeing a slave, and it is permissible (to free) a Muslim and a non-Muslim.' The source is the Quran, Surah al-Mujadilah.
The ruling is part of the chapter on expiation.
2
Understand the term 'raqabah'
The commentator explains that 'raqabah' refers to the whole person, not just the neck, and is used absolutely in the Quran and Sunnah.
This is a common linguistic interpretation in Hanafi fiqh.
3
Analyze the proof
The absence of any qualification (qayd) in the Quranic verse makes the command absolute. Therefore, any slave, regardless of religion, gender, age, or minor physical defects, is valid.
Hanafi usul: al-mutlaq yajri ala itlaqihi.
4
Recognize the limitation
The absolute is restricted by the principle of 'haml al-mutlaq ala al-fard al-kamil'. A slave must be sound and capable of providing benefit; a blind or insane person is excluded.
The limitation is not from a textual restriction but from the essence of the term.
5
Note the cross-madhhab difference
Shafi'i restricts the slave to a Muslim slave, while Hanafi permits non-Muslim. The Hanafi proof is the absolute wording.
This is a practical difference in fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في الكفارة: عتق رقبة، وجاز فيها المسلم والكافر. [١] قوله : رقبة؛ الرقبة - بفتحات - ، وإن كان اسماً للعضو الخاص، لكنه مما يعبر به عن الكل وقد كثر استعماله في الكتاب والسنّة في الذات، والتنوين فيه للوحدة؛ أي عتق مملوك من مماليكه. (٢) قوله : وجاز فيها ... الخ ؛ الأصل فيه: أنّ الرقبة المذكورة في القرآن في كفارة الظهار مطلقة، غير مقيدة بالإسلام، ولا بالذكورة، ولا بغير ذلك، فيجوز عتق كافر و مسلم وصبي وبالغ ، وذكر وأنثى، والأعور وغير الأعور، ومقطوع إحدى اليدين والرجلين. فإن قلت : فينبغي أن يجوز الأعمى والمجنون وغيرهما ما سيصرح المصنف بعدم جوازه. قلت : الرقبة وإن كانت مطلقة لكنّها مقيّدة بالسلامة، فإنّ المطلق يحمل على الفرد الكامل، فلا يجزئ ما كان ناقصاً في كونه رقبة، وهو فائتُ جنس المنفعة، هذا الأصل. كذا حققه الشارح له في تنقيح الأصول).
Chapter on Expiation: Freeing a slave, and it is permissible (to free) a Muslim and a non-Muslim. [1] His saying: 'A slave' - al-raqabah (with fatha) - even though it is a term for a specific body part, but it is used to refer to the whole, and its use in the Book and Sunnah for the self is frequent. The tanween indicates singularity, i.e., freeing a owned person from his slaves. [2] His saying: 'And it is permissible in it (the slave)...' etc.; the basis for this is that the slave mentioned in the Quran in the expiation of dhihar is absolute (muṭlaq), not restricted by Islam, nor by masculinity, nor by anything else. So it is permissible to free a non-Muslim, a Muslim, a boy, an adult, male and female, a one-eyed person, a person with a hand or foot cut off. If you say: It should be permissible (to free) a blind person, a madman, and others that the author will explicitly state are not valid. I say: Even though the slave is absolute, it is restricted by soundness, because the absolute is understood to refer to the perfect individual. So what is deficient in being a slave, i.e., lacking the essence of benefit, is not sufficient. This is the principle. As the commentator verified in Tanqih al-Usul.
Source pages p. 416

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on the expiation (kafaarah) for dhihar. The matn states that the expiation is freeing a slave, and it is valid to free either a Muslim or a non-Muslim slave. The commentator first explains the legal meaning of 'raqabah' (slave) as referring to the whole person. Then he discusses the ruling: since the Quranic verse for dhihar expiation (Surah al-Mujadilah) mentions 'raqabah' without any qualification, it is absolute. Therefore, any slave who is a sound and complete individual suffices, regardless of religion, gender, age, or minor physical defects like being one-eyed or missing a limb. However, the absolute must be restricted to a sound slave because the principle is that when the Shariah gives an absolute command, it is interpreted as the perfect type. Thus, a slave who is blind, insane, or otherwise completely deficient (like missing essential benefit) is not valid. The commentator refers to the principle of 'haml al-mutlaq ala al-fard al-kamil' (taking the absolute to mean the perfect individual). This is a key usul principle from the Hanafi school.

Key Terms
رقبة (raqabah): A slave, the whole person. Used absolutely in the Quran for expiation.
فرد كامل (fard kāmil): The perfect individual; the ideal type to which an absolute command is applied.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) A minor

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school upholds the absolute wording of the Quran regarding the slave for dhihar expiation, requiring only soundness, not faith or gender.

Non-Muslim Slave in Dhihar: Hanafi vs Shafi'i
Inter-madhhab dispute on whether absolute is restricted by muqayyad in another ruling

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school allows a non-Muslim slave in dhihar expiation and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument based on the usul principle of mutlaq and muqayyad.

Core Idea
A mutlaq is not restricted by a muqayyad from a different ruling unless textually linked.

This lesson examines the dispute between the Hanafi school and Imam al-Shafi'i over the validity of freeing a non-Muslim slave for dhihar expiation. The disagreement stems from a fundamental difference in usul al-fiqh: how to treat an absolute (mutlaq) term when a restricted (muqayyad) term appears in a separate but similar ruling. Understanding this dispute clarifies why the two schools reach different fiqh conclusions and illustrates the practical impact of usul principles.

Guided Path
1
1. State the Hanafi position
The Hanafi school holds that freeing a non-Muslim slave is valid for dhihar expiation. Identify the Quranic text they rely on (the absolute 'raqabah' in Surah al-Mujadilah).
This is based on leaving the absolute as is, since the verse does not specify a believing slave.
2
2. Identify the Shafi'i position and its proof
Imam al-Shafi'i argues that only a believing slave is valid. He cites the muqayyad 'raqabah mu'minah' from the accidental killing expiation (Surah al-Nisa'). Note his usul principle: when mutlaq and muqayyad appear in the same type of ruling (expiations), the muqayyad restricts the mutlaq.
The key is that he considers all expiations as a single ruling category.
3
3. Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response rejects this because the incidents (dhihar vs. accidental killing) are different rulings. The Hanafi usul principle: mutlaq and muqayyad in separate rulings are independent and the mutlaq remains absolute unless a textual link joins them.
This difference is rooted in the Hanafi vs. Shafi'i usul on combining mutlaq and muqayyad.
4
4. Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: Hanafis allow a non-Muslim slave, Shafi'is do not. This affects expiation options for a person who only has a non-Muslim slave or for whom freeing a non-Muslim is easier.
The dispute has real-world implications for those performing expiation.
5
5. Evaluate the tarjih
Within the Hanafi school, the position that a non-Muslim slave is valid is mufta bihi, based on the principle that the absolute remains until restricted by a textual link in the same ruling.
This shows the consistency of Hanafi usul.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[٣]قوله : وفيه خلافُ الشافعي الله ؛ فإنّه لا يجزئ عنده الكافر، ومنشأ الخلافِ أن المطلق والمقيد إذا ورد في حكمٍ واحدٍ يحملُ عنده المطلق على المقيد، وإن اختلفت الحادثة، وقد ذكر في القرآن في كفارة الظهار في سورة المجادلة، وفي كفارة اليمين في سورة المائدة : تحرير رقبة، وفي كفّارة القتل خطأ في سورة النساء : تحرير رقبة مؤمنة، فتقيّد الرقبة المطلقة بالمؤمنة.
[3] His saying: 'And in it (the matter) there is a dispute with al-Shafi'i (may Allah be pleased with him): For according to him, a non-Muslim is not valid. The origin of the dispute is that when an absolute (mutlaq) and a restricted (muqayyad) occur in a single ruling, he (al-Shafi'i) holds that the absolute is interpreted in light of the restricted, even if the incidents are different. And it has been mentioned in the Quran in the expiation of dhihar in Surah al-Mujadilah, and in the expiation of an oath in Surah al-Ma'idah: 'freeing a slave', and in the expiation of accidental killing in Surah al-Nisa': 'freeing a believing slave'. Thus the absolute 'slave' becomes restricted to 'believing'.
Source pages p. 416

Commentary
This passage presents the inter-madhhab dispute between the Hanafi school and al-Shafi'i regarding the validity of freeing a non-Muslim slave in the expiation of dhihar. The Hanafi position, as established in the previous discussion, is that it is valid because the Quranic command is absolute. However, al-Shafi'i holds that a non-Muslim slave is not valid. The basis of his argument is a usul principle: when an absolute (mutlaq) and a restricted (muqayyad) form are found in the Shariah for the same ruling, the absolute is interpreted as restricted. Here, the verses on expiation for dhihar and for breaking an oath say 'freeing a slave' (absolute), while the verse for accidental killing says 'freeing a believing slave' (muqayyad). Since all are expiations, al-Shafi'i argues that the restriction from the killing verse applies to the other expiations as well. The Hanafi school rejects this because the incidents are different, and the absolute remains unless the restriction is in a single ruling. Hanafi usul holds that mutlaq and muqayyad are not combined across different rulings without a textual link.

Key Terms
مطلق (muṭlaq): An absolute term without qualification, referring to the generality of the thing.
مقيد (muqayyad): A restricted term with a qualification, e.g., 'believing slave'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does Imam al-Shafi'i not allow a non-Muslim slave for dhihar expiation?
A) He believes the absolute must be restricted by a muqayyad from the same type of ruling
B) He considers non-Muslim slaves invalid in all expiations
C) He rejects the Quranic verse
D) He follows a different hadith

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits freeing a non-Muslim slave for dhihar expiation because, in their usul, a mutlaq is not restricted by a muqayyad from a different ruling.

Freeing a Non-Muslim Slave in Kaffarah: Hanafi vs Shafi'i Dispute
Explaining the Hanafi ruling on freeing a non-Muslim slave in kaffarah and the dispute with Shafi'i.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on freeing a non-Muslim slave in kaffarah and explain the usul principle underlying the dispute with the Shafi'i school.

Core Idea
The absolute term 'raqaba' in kaffarah is not restricted by the faith condition from a different incident.

This passage addresses a key inter-madhhab dispute: can a non-Muslim slave be freed in kaffarat al-zihar and al-yamin? The Hanafi school, based on usul al-fiqh, holds that the absolute 'raqaba' remains unrestricted unless the same ruling and same incident require restriction. The Shafi'i school applies the opposite principle. Understanding this dispute illuminates the importance of mutlaq and muqayyad categories in Hanafi jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Hanafi position
State which slaves are valid for kaffarah according to the Hanafi school. Note that the text says 'Jazā fīhā al-muslim wa al-kāfir' – both Muslim and non-Muslim are permissible.
The condition is that the slave must be sound (lacking defects that negate jins al-manfa'ah).
2
Identify the Shafi'i objection
Imam al-Shafi'i requires the slave to be a believer (mu'min/mu'minah). Determine his proof from the Quran.
He cites the verse in Surah al-Nisa' (4:92) which specifies 'raqabah mu'minah' for qatl al-khatā'.
3
Trace the Hanafi usul principle
The Hanafi school argues that mutlaq and muqayyad from different incidents (zihar, yamin, qatl) do not interact; each ruling retains its own condition. The absolute 'raqaba' in zihar and yamin remains unrestricted by faith.
This principle is detailed in works like al-Tawdih and al-Talwih.
4
Analyze the proof from Quran
Compare the verses for zihar (Surah al-Mujadala), yamin (Surah al-Ma'idah 5:89), and qatl (Surah al-Nisa' 4:92). Note the presence or absence of 'mu'minah'.
The Hanafis argue that each verse is a separate ruling; the Shafi'is argue that all kaffarah constitute one ruling.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Identify the practical consequence: Hanafis allow freeing a non-Muslim slave for zihar and yamin; Shafi'is do not. This affects availability and cost of expiation.
This is the real-world impact of the usul difference.

And it has been mentioned in the Qur'an in kaffarat al-zihar in Surat al-Mujadala, and in kaffarat al-yamin in Surat al-Ma'idah: 'freeing a raqaba', and in kaffarat qatl al-khata' in Surat al-Nisa': 'freeing a believing raqaba', so the absolute raqaba is restricted by the believing one.

Book of Divorce / Chapter of Zihar: and the male and the female, the young and the old, the deaf, the one-eyed, and the one whose one hand and one foot are cut off from opposite sides, and a slave who has not paid anything. [1] And its verification is in Usul al-Fiqh regarding carrying the absolute upon the restricted. (And the male and the female, (2) the young and the old, the deaf: i.e., one who has heaviness in his ears; as for one who does not hear at all, it is not permissible, because he is lacking the type of benefit. And the one-eyed and the one whose one hand and one foot are cut off from opposite sides, and a slave who has not paid anything. And according to us: the absolute is acted upon with its absoluteness, and the restricted is acted upon with its restriction. And the proofs of both sides, with what supports them and what counts against them, are detailed in 'al-Tawdih' and 'al-Talwih' and others. (4) (+) [1] His statement: 'al-mutlaq'; he said in 'al-Talwih': it is the common in its genus, meaning that its share of the reality includes many shares without comprehensiveness and specification. And 'al-muqayyad' is what removes from commonality in some way: like 'believing raqaba', which removes from the commonality of believing and otherwise, even if it is common among believing slaves. (2) His statement: 'and the deaf'; likewise permissible is the castrated, the one whose ears are cut off, or whose eyebrows are gone, or whose nose is cut off, and the like. [3] His statement: 'the one lacking the type of benefit'; i.e., the benefit of sight, hearing, speech, strength, movement, and intellect, and what is meant by it is the total loss of benefit; i.e., the intended benefit from the slave. Thus in the commentaries of 'al-Hidayah' and others. [4] His statement: 'from opposite sides'; meaning that his right hand and left foot are cut off, or the opposite, such that he is able to walk.
Source pages p. 416

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools regarding the permissibility of freeing a non-Muslim slave in kaffarah (expiation). The author begins by stating the Hanafi position: in kaffarat al-zihar, the slave to be freed can be Muslim or non-Muslim, male or female, provided the slave is sound and possesses the general benefit (jins al-manfa'ah). The term 'raqaba' in the Qur'an is absolute (mutlaq) and not restricted to believing slaves, so any slave qualifies. However, if the slave is blind, insane, or lacks a complete sense like hearing, such a slave is not valid because they are 'fā'it jins al-manfa'ah' (lacking the type of benefit). The author then addresses the Shafi'i objection: Imam al-Shafi'i requires the slave to be a believer (mu'minah) because in kaffarat qatl al-khatā' the Qur'an specifies 'raqaba mu'minah'. His principle is that when an absolute and a restricted term occur in the same ruling (i.e., for the same type of act), the absolute is restricted to the restricted. Since all three kaffarahs involve freeing a slave, the restriction (faith) from one applies to all. The Hanafi response, based on their usul al-fiqh, is that the absolute remains absolute and the restricted remains restricted unless they occur in the same ruling and same incident. Here, the ruling (kaffarah) is the same type, but the incidents (zihar, yamin, qatl) are different, so each verse retains its own condition. Thus, the Hanafi school holds that the absolute 'raqaba' in zihar and yamin is not restricted by the 'mu'minah' in qatl. The passage then lists additional permissible and impermissible defects in the slave, emphasizing that the slave must have the type of benefit intact.

Key Terms
رقبة (raqaba): Literally 'neck', but metaphorically means a slave (the whole person). In this context, it refers to a slave to be freed.
مطلق (mutlaq): An absolute term that is common in its genus, without restriction. Here, 'raqaba' without qualification.
مقيد (muqayyad): A restricted term, here 'raqaba mu'minah' (believing slave).
فائت جنس المنفعة (fā'it jins al-manfa'ah): Lacking the type of benefit; meaning a slave who is blind, deaf, insane, etc., missing a major sense or limb that prevents full utility.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, can a non-Muslim slave be freed in kaffarat al-zihar?
A) Yes, because the verse is absolute.
B) No, because all kaffarah require a believing slave.
C) Yes, but only if the slave is a male.
D) No, unless the slave is healthy.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school maintains that an absolute term (mutlaq) is not restricted by a restricted term (muqayyad) from a different incident, allowing non-Muslim slaves in most kaffarah.

Conditions for a Slave to Be Valid in Zihar Expiation
Explaining which slaves are valid for expiation in Zihar

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which a freed slave is valid for zihar expiation and identify which disabilities or defects invalidate the expiation.

Core Idea
Only total loss of a major intended benefit invalidates a slave for zihar expiation; mere impairment is permissible.

In zihar expiation, the husband must free a slave before returning to his wife. But not every slave qualifies. The Hanafi school details precise conditions: the slave must be human, free from total loss of senses or limbs essential for benefit. This lesson unpacks which slaves are valid and which are not, based on the principle of 'fā'it jins al-manfa'ah'.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is freeing a slave for zihar kafarah. The ruling is that it must satisfy certain conditions for validity.
The ruling is on the validity of the expiation, not on the act of freeing itself.
2
List the general permissibility conditions
The slave must be human (male/female, young/old) and must not have lost the entire type of intended benefit (jins al-manfa'ah). Certain categories like mudabbar, umm walad, and mukatib who has paid part are invalid due to diminished slavery.
Intended benefits include sight, hearing, speech, strength, walking, and intellect.
3
Distinguish total loss from mere impairment
Apply the examples: deaf with some hearing (valid) vs. totally deaf (invalid); one-eyed (valid) vs. blind (invalid); one hand and opposite leg cut (valid) vs. both hands or both feet cut (invalid).
The key is whether the slave can still derive some benefit from the impaired sense/limb.
4
Identify the exceptions (mawani')
Mudabbar (promised freedom after death), umm walad (mother of child), and mukatib who has paid part are invalid because their slavery is weakened, and freeing them is not a pure act of worship.
A mukatib who has paid nothing is valid because slavery is still complete.
5
Apply the rule to special cases
Buying a close relative with intention of expiation results in freedom upon purchase and is valid. Freeing half a slave then the rest is also valid if done before intercourse.
These cases are derived from the same principle of complete ownership.

So the blind loses the benefit of sight, the one-eyed impairs it, the one whose two hands or two feet or a hand and a foot from one side are cut loses the benefit of walking, unlike the one whose one hand or one foot is cut or the one whose hand and foot are cut from opposite sides, for walking is possible there. And the one whose two thumbs of the hands are cut loses the type of benefit because strength of grip is with them, unlike the two thumbs of the feet. And not a mudabbar etc.; the reason for the non-validity of the mudabbar, and similarly umm walad, is that they have become entitled to freedom through tadbir and istilad, so slavery in them is defective. And the reason for the non-validity of the mukatib who has paid some of the consideration is that his freeing is not a pure act of worship but is for compensation, unlike the case where he has not paid anything, for his freeing is not for compensation, and slavery in him is complete as long as one dirham remains on him. i.e., before intercourse with the one from whom he has made zihar; because he freed him with two statements and the deficiency is settled in his ownership due to the cause of expiation; and that does not prevent permissibility. Because the strength of grip is with them, so with their loss the type of benefit is lost. [End of text]
Source pages p. 417

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the conditions of slaves valid for expiation (kafarah) in zihar. The author explains that the slave to be freed must be a person (male or female, young or old) and must not have lost the entire type of intended benefit (jins al-manfa'ah). The deaf (asamm) is allowed if he can hear something, but if totally deaf, it's invalid. The one-eyed (a'war) and one with a missing hand and leg from opposite sides (mukhtalif) are allowed because they can still walk. A mukatib who hasn't paid anything is allowed because his slavery is still complete. The text then distinguishes between absolute (mutlaq) and restricted (muqayyad) terms in usul al-fiqh, saying that each works according to its own condition; the restricted term in killing expiation is not carried over to zihar. The key point is that invalidity occurs only when the entire type of benefit is lost, not just impairment. Examples: blind (a'ma), insane without lucid intervals, both hands cut, both thumbs of hands cut, both feet cut, or one hand and one foot from the same side. A mudabbar (promised freedom after death) and umm walad (mother of child) are invalid because their slavery is weakened. A mukatib who has paid part is invalid because freeing him is not purely for Allah (it compensates the master). Buying a close relative with intention of expiation results in freedom upon purchase and is valid. Freeing half a slave then the rest is also valid if done before intercourse.

Key Terms
فائت جنس المنفعة (Fā'it jins al-manfa'ah): One who has lost the entire type of intended benefit from a slave, i.e., a major function like sight, hearing, speech, strength, walking, or intellect.
مطلق (Muṭlaq): A term that is unrestricted in its generality, applying to all individuals of a genus without specification.
مقيد (Muqayyad): A term that is restricted by a qualification, such as 'believing slave' restricting the general 'slave'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Only the complete loss of a major intended benefit (sight, hearing, speech, strength, walking, or intellect) invalidates a slave for zihar expiation; mere impairment or decreased slavery (mudabbar, partial mukatib) are invalid for other reasons.

Freeing Half a Slave for Zihar Expiation: The Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Divisibility of Manumission
Intra-Hanafi dispute on partial manumission for kaffarah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on whether freeing half a slave suffices for zihar expiation and explain the disagreement between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
Freeing half a slave does not fulfill zihar expiation — manumission is divisible, so only a complete slave counts.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah addresses a critical intra-Hanafi dispute: does freeing half a slave, whether jointly owned or one's own, count as expiation for zihar? The answer depends on whether manumission (itq) is considered divisible or indivisible. We will explore two scenarios and the reasoning behind Imam Abu Hanifa's position, which is the relied-upon view.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The passage presents two cases: (1) freeing half of a jointly owned slave and then guaranteeing the other half, and (2) freeing half of one's own slave with intention of expiation, then having intercourse, then freeing the other half.
2
State Abu Hanifa's ruling
According to Abu Hanifa, neither scenario fulfills the expiation requirement because manumission is divisible; freeing half does not free the whole, so a complete slave is not freed.
3
State the Sahibayn's ruling
The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) hold that manumission is indivisible; freeing any part frees the entire slave, so freeing half counts as freeing the whole and suffices for expiation.
4
Understand the key concept
The dispute hinges on tajzi'at al-itq (divisibility of manumission). Abu Hanifa sees manumission as divisible; the Sahibayn see it as indivisible.
5
Identify the practical consequence
The thamarah al-khilaf: Under Abu Hanifa, expiation is invalid and must be repeated with a complete slave; under the Sahibayn, expiation is valid if the manumitter is solvent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإعتاق نصف عبد مشترك، ثم باقيه بعد ضمانه، ونصف عبده عن تكفيره، ثم باقيه بعد وطء من ظاهر منها. قوله: وإعتاق...الخ؛ يعني إن أعتق نصف عبد مشترك بينه وبين الآخر، وهو موسر فضمن نصف شريكه وملكه بالضمان فأعتق لا يجزئ مثل هذا العتق عن كفارته. قوله: لأنه...الخ؛ حاصله أنه لما أعتق نصفه صار النصف الآخر الذي هو في ملك صاحبه ذا نقصان وعيب لتعدّر استدامة الرقبة فيه بسبب حرمة النصف، ولهذا يجب على المعتق ضمانه، وإن كان موسراً وعلى العبد السعاية بقدر قيمة نصفه ليكون كله حراً، وبعدما يصير ناقصاً ينتقل إلى ملك المعتق بسبب الضمان، فلا يجزئ مثل هذا عن الكفارة. قوله: بخلاف...الخ؛ قال في «العناية»: فإن قيل: يجب أن يقع عن الكفارة عندهما، وإن كان المعتق معسراً؛ لأنه يصير حراً مديوناً بناء على أن الإعتاق عندهما لا يتجزئ. أجيب: بأنه لم يجز؛ لأن وجوب هذا الدين بسبب الإعتاق، فلا يكون هذا العتق مجاناً فلا يقع عن الكفارة. قوله: ونصف...الخ؛ أي لا يتجزئ أن يعتق نصف عبده بنية الكفارة، ثم يطأ الزوجة التي ظاهر منها، ثم يعتق نصفه الباقي؛ لأن الواجب أن يعتق الرقبة كلها قبل الوطء، وهذا عنده بناء على أن العتق يتجزئ عنده، وعندهما العتق لا يتجزئ، فيكون إعتاق النصف إعتاق الكل، فيحصل الكل قبل المسيس.
And freeing half of a jointly owned slave, then the remainder after guaranteeing it, and half of his slave for his expiation, then the remainder after intercourse of the one who performed zihar from her. He said: 'And freeing...etc.' means if he freed half of a jointly owned slave between him and another, and he is wealthy, so he guaranteed the half of his partner and owned it by guarantee, then freed, such freeing does not suffice for his expiation. He said: 'Because...etc.'; its summary: when he freed half, the other half that belongs to his partner became defective due to the impossibility of continuing slavery in it because of the prohibition of half, hence guarantee is owed, and the slave must work to pay off half his value to become fully free, after which the defective part transfers to the owner by guarantee, so such freeing does not suffice for expiation. He said: 'Contrary to...etc.'; he said in al-'Inayah: 'If it is said: it should count for expiation according to them (the two disciples) even if the manumitter is insolvent because he becomes a free debtor based on the principle that manumission is indivisible according to them.' The answer: it is not permissible because the obligation of this debt is due to the manumission, so this manumission is not gratuitous and thus does not count for expiation. He said: 'And half...etc.'; meaning it is not valid to free half of his slave with intention of expiation, then have intercourse with the wife from whom he performed zihar, then free the remaining half; because the obligation is to free the entire slave before intercourse, and this is according to his (Abu Hanifa's) view that manumission is divisible, while according to them (the two disciples) manumission is indivisible, so freeing half is freeing the whole, and the whole occurs before intercourse.
Source pages p. 419

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah discusses the ruling on expiation for zihar through manumission, specifically when the slave is partially owned. The matn states two scenarios: (1) freeing half of a jointly owned slave and then guaranteeing the other half, and (2) freeing half of one's own slave with intention of expiation, then having intercourse, then freeing the remaining half. The commentary explains that according to Imam Abu Hanifa, such partial manumission does not fulfill the expiation requirement because manumission is divisible; only a complete slave counts. For the joint slave, freeing half makes the other half defective, so the manumitter must guarantee it, and the slave must work to pay for his freedom, resulting in a transaction rather than pure charity. For half of one's own slave, freeing half and then keeping the other half for later intercourse does not fulfill the obligation of freeing a complete slave before intercourse. However, the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) hold that manumission is indivisible: freeing any part frees the whole slave. Therefore, according to them, freeing half of a jointly owned slave, if the manumitter is wealthy and can guarantee the other half, results in full ownership and thus full manumission, counting for expiation. Similarly, freeing half of one's own slave counts as freeing the whole, so if done before intercourse, expiation is fulfilled. The text also addresses the objection regarding an insolvent manumitter: according to the Sahibayn, even if insolvent, the slave becomes free and owes the partner's share, but the Hanafi response is that this debt arises from the manumission, making it non-gratuitous, so it does not count for expiation. The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position in the Hanafi school is that manumission is divisible (Abu Hanifa's view), so partial manumission does not suffice.

Key Terms
عتق (itq): Manumission; freeing a slave. In this context, it refers to the act required as expiation for zihar.
ضمان (daman): Guarantee; financial liability for the value of the partner's share in a jointly owned slave.
تجزئة العتق (tajzi'at al-itq): Divisibility of manumission; whether freeing a part of a slave frees the whole or only that part.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

Conditions of Fasting Expiation and the Partial Emancipation Dispute
Ruling on fasting expiation and intra-Hanafi dispute on partial emancipation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for a valid fasting expiation in dhihar and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute on partial emancipation, including the practical consequence of each view.

Core Idea
Emancipation is divisible for Abu Hanifa, so freeing half does not fulfill expiation; the Sahibayn hold it is indivisible.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah presents two key points: the precise conditions for the fasting alternative in dhihar expiation, and a nuanced intra-Hanafi disagreement on whether freeing part of a slave counts as freeing the whole. The author defines 'consecutive' fasting and its exceptions, then raises a scenario where a man frees only half a slave before intercourse. Understanding the difference between Abu Hanifa's view and that of his two disciples is essential for correct fatwa and practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the primary ruling on fasting expiation
The passage states that if a man who performed dhihar cannot free a slave, he must fast two consecutive months. Note the conditions: no Ramadan and no five prohibited days (Eid al-Fitr, Eid al-Adha, and three days of Tashriq) should be included.
The fasting must be completed before intercourse.
2
Define 'consecutive' (wala')
The commentator explains that consecutiveness is broken by a gap of twenty-nine days or more. Gaps of less than twenty-nine days do not break it. Also, if Ramadan or the prohibited days occur during the fast, the fast must be restarted.
This definition is specific to the Hanafi school.
3
Analyze the disputed scenario of partial emancipation
The text presents a case where a man frees half a slave before intercourse, intending to free the other half later. According to Abu Hanifa, this is invalid because emancipation is divisible (تجزئ), so freeing half is not freeing the whole. The condition of complete emancipation before intercourse is not met.
The two disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that emancipation is indivisible, so freeing half suffices for the whole.
4
Contrast the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn
Abu Hanifa's view is based on the principle that the obligation is to free a complete slave (الرقبة كلها). The Sahibayn consider the act of emancipation as a single indivisible act; thus any part suffices.
The text does not cite a specific hadith; the difference is one of legal reasoning (tatbiq al-qiyas).
5
Determine the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
If a man frees half a slave before intercourse, according to Abu Hanifa he must still free the other half before intercourse becomes permissible. According to the Sahibayn, intercourse is permissible immediately after freeing half.
This difference affects whether the expiation is fulfilled or must be completed.

Source Text

And if he is unable to free a slave, he fasts two consecutive months, in which there is no Ramadan and no five days the fasting of which is prohibited. It must be before intercourse, but according to them (the two disciples) it is permissible because freeing part is freeing the whole according to them. [1]- [Y] And if he is unable to free a slave, he fasts two consecutive months, in which there is no Ramadan and no five days the fasting of which is prohibited. He has intercourse with the wife from whom he has performed dhihar, then he frees the remaining half, because the obligation is to free the entire slave before intercourse. This according to him (Abu Hanifa) is based on the fact that emancipation is divisible in his view, while according to them emancipation is not divisible, so freeing half is freeing the whole, and the whole is achieved before intercourse. [1] His statement: 'And if he is unable' means the one who performs dhihar is unable to free a slave, i.e., he cannot find a slave or its price. [2] His statement: 'consecutively' means in succession, not separated by breaking the fast for twenty-nine days. [3] His statement: 'and no five days the fasting of which is prohibited' are the day of Fitr, the day of Adha, and the days of Tashriq. And in al-Fatawa al-Hindiyya: 'If he expiates by fasting and breaks a day due to an excuse of illness or travel, he restarts the fast; likewise if the day of Fitr, the day of Nahr, or the days of Tashriq occur, he restarts the fast; and if he fasts these days and does not break, he also restarts.'
Source pages p. 420

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute about the ruling on expiation for dhihar. The main text states the ruling: if a man who has declared dhihar cannot free a slave, he must fast two consecutive months, excluding Ramadan and the five prohibited days (Eid al-Fitr, Eid al-Adha, and the three days of Tashriq). This fasting must be completed before he has intercourse with his wife. The commentator then addresses a specific scenario: what if a man frees only half a slave before intercourse and then later frees the other half? According to Abu Hanifa, this is not valid because he considers emancipation to be divisible; thus, freeing half is not freeing the whole, so the condition of freeing a complete slave before intercourse is not met. However, according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the two disciples), emancipation is indivisible; freeing part is considered freeing the whole, so the condition is satisfied. The commentary further defines 'consecutive' as not having a gap of twenty-nine days and clarifies that if the fast is broken by the entry of Ramadan or on the prohibited days, one must restart, as fasting those days would break the consecutiveness per the relied-upon view.

Key Terms
ولاء (wala'): Consecutive days of fasting without a gap of twenty-nine days or more.
عتق (itq): Emancipation of a slave, a required act of expiation for dhihar.
تجزئ (tajazzu'): Divisibility of a legal act; here, whether freeing part of a slave is legally effective.

The expiation for dhihar by fasting requires two consecutive months without a gap of 29+ days, and emancipation as expiation is subject to an intra-Hanafi dispute on whether partial freeing suffices.

Breaking Consecutiveness in Dhihar Expiation Fast
Ruling statement on what breaks the consecutive fast for dhihar expiation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the acts that break the consecutive fast of dhihar expiation and apply the rule that any break requires restarting the fast.

Core Idea
Any break in the dhihar expiation fast, excused or not, forces a restart.

This lesson examines the strict requirement of consecutiveness (tataabu') in the two-month fast for dhihar expiation. The text from the classical Hanafi work 'Umda al-Ri'aya explains that both excused and unexcused breaks, as well as certain types of intercourse, break the consecutiveness and necessitate restarting the entire fast. Understanding this rule is crucial for correctly performing this obligatory expiation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its condition
The act is the two-month consecutive fast for dhihar expiation. The key condition is consecutiveness (tataabu'): the two months must be fasted without a single missed day.
Without consecutiveness, the fast does not fulfill the expiation.
2
List the events that break consecutiveness
The text lists: breaking the fast due to an excuse (travel, illness, postpartum), breaking without an excuse, and intercourse with the wife at night intentionally or during the day forgetfully. All these break the sequence.
Unexcused breaks are even more obviously breaks, as per the primary route reasoning.
3
Apply the consequence: restart the fast
Whenever consecutiveness is broken, the person must restart the entire two-month fast from day one (isti'naf). The previous days of fasting do not count.
This applies even if the break was due to a valid excuse.
4
Distinguish the feeding alternative
If the person was feeding sixty poor people instead of fasting, and he has intercourse during that period, he does NOT restart the feeding. He continues from where he left off.
Feeding does not require consecutiveness, so intercourse does not break it.
5
Note the gender-specific rules
For a woman fasting for dhihar or other expiations, menstruation does NOT break consecutiveness in some cases (e.g., expiation for killing), but postpartum bleeding does. She must connect the days after menstruation to those before.
This is because she cannot find two months free from menstruation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

[1]

(۲)
وعليها أن تصل ما بعد الحيض بما قبله ، فلو أفطرت بعده يوماً استقبلت لتركها التتابع بلا ضرورة، بخلاف النفاس فإنّه أمر يقع أحياناً، فيقطع التتابع في صوم كلّ كفارة، وصوم ثلاثة أيام في كفارة اليمين يقطع تتابعه حيضها أيضاً. كذا في «البحر» [٢]قوله : أو بغيره ؛ أي أفطر بغير العذر، وهذا تصريح بما علم منه سابقاً بالطريق الأولى، فإنّه لَمَّا كان الإفطار بعذر قاطعاً للتتابع كان الإفطار بغير عذر قاطعاً بالطريق الأولى. As for her menstruation, it does not break consecutiveness in her fast for killing her breaking the fast, because she cannot find two months free from it. And she must join what comes after menstruation to what came before it. If she breaks the fast after it for one day, she restarts due to leaving consecutiveness without necessity, unlike postpartum bleeding, which occurs occasionally, so it breaks the consecutiveness in the fast of every expiation. And the fast of three days in the expiation of an oath, her menstruation also breaks its consecutiveness. So it is in al-Bahr. [2] His statement: 'or without an excuse' means he breaks the fast without an excuse, and this is an explicit statement of what was known before by the primary route. For when breaking with an excuse breaks consecutiveness, then breaking without an excuse breaks it by the primary route. [3] His statement: 'or he has intercourse with her' means the wife from whom he performed dhihar during the two months. If he has intercourse with another woman during them, then if it is intercourse that invalidates the fast, like intercourse during the day intentionally, it breaks consecutiveness and he must restart by agreement. And if it does not invalidate, like intercourse during the day forgetfully or at night in any case, it does not break consecutiveness and he is not required to restart by agreement. So it is in al-Nihaya. [4] His statement: 'intentionally' this restriction is agreed upon, for intercourse at night, intentionally and forgetfully are the same. And the restriction of intercourse during the day to forgetfulness is because if he has intercourse with her during the day intentionally, he restarts by agreement. So it is in al-'Inayah. [0] His statement: 'not the feeding' means if he approaches the wife from whom he performed dhihar during the feeding of sixty poor people.
Source pages p. 421

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement on what breaks the consecutive nature of the two-month fast for dhihar expiation. The main text says: if the fasting person breaks his fast with or without a legitimate excuse (like travel or illness), or if he has intercourse with his wife during the two months (at night intentionally or during the day forgetfully), he must restart the fast from the beginning. However, if he has intercourse during the feeding expiation (i.e., feeding sixty poor people), he does not need to restart the feeding; he continues from where he left off. The commentary elaborates: 'excuse' includes travel, illness, and for a woman, postpartum bleeding (nifas) which breaks the fast and the consecutiveness. Menstruation (hayd) does not break consecutiveness for a woman fasting for the expiation of killing (not dhihar) because she cannot find two months free from menstruation; she must connect the days after menstruation to those before. For dhihar, the same rule applies? The commentary specifies that in the expiation for killing, menstruation does not break consecutiveness. The passage also discusses intercourse: if the man has intercourse with his wife during the day intentionally, it invalidates the fast and breaks consecutiveness; if forgetfully, it does not. Intercourse at night never invalidates the fast, so it does not break consecutiveness. The key point is that breaking the consecutiveness (tataabu') requires restarting the fast from the beginning, but this does not apply to the feeding expiation.

Key Terms
عذر (udhr): A legitimate excuse such as travel, illness, or postpartum bleeding that permits breaking the fast but still requires restarting.
تتابع (tataabu'): Consecutiveness of days in fasting; breaking it requires starting over.
استئناف (isti'naf): To restart the fast from the beginning due to breaking consecutiveness.

Key Takeaway
Any break in the two-month dhihar expiation fast—excused or unexcused, including certain intercourse—requires restarting the entire fast from the beginning.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Restarting Fast in Zihar Kaffarah
Intra-Hanafi dispute on restarting fast after intercourse during zihar expiation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad versus Abu Yusuf regarding restarting the two-month fast after intercourse, and explain the condition of precedence.

Core Idea
If intercourse occurs during the two-month fast, even forgetfully by day, the fast must be restarted – unless you follow Abu Yusuf.

This passage from 'Umda al-Ri'aya' details an internal Hanafi dispute on the effect of intercourse during the two-month fast of zihar expiation. The majority view of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad requires restarting the fast, while Abu Yusuf disagrees. Understanding this ikhtilaf reveals the precise conditions governing the expiation sequence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue
Determine the scenario under dispute: intercourse during the two-month fast of zihar expiation, specifically when the fast is not invalidated (e.g., forgetful intercourse by day or intercourse at night).
The fast remains technically valid but the condition of precedence may be broken.
2
State the positions
Abu Hanifa and Muhammad hold that any such intercourse requires restarting the fast from the beginning. Abu Yusuf holds that if the fast remains valid, no restart is needed.
3
Analyze the proofs
Abu Hanifa and Muhammad argue that the condition is strict: all fasting must precede intercourse. Abu Yusuf argues that since continuity is maintained and some fasting does precede intercourse, restarting would put all fasting after intercourse, which is worse.
The dispute hinges on the interpretation of 'min qabli an yatamassa' (before they touch each other).
4
Determine the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position is that of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, as it is more precautionary and aligns with the literal condition.
This is the position applied in fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الطلاق الظهار
وإن أفطر بعذر، أو بغيره، أو وطئها في الشهرين ليلاً عمداً، أو يوماً سهواً، استأنف الصوم لا الإطعام إن وطئها في خلاله.
[1] قوله: بعذر؛ كسفر أو مرض أو نفاسها في كفّارة القتل خطأ، وكفارة إفطار صوم رمضان، وأما حيضها فلا يقطعُ التتابع في صومها عن قتلها إفطارها؛ لأنها لا تجد شهرين خاليين عنه.
وعليها أن تصل ما بعد الحيض بما قبله، فلو أفطرت بعده يوماً استقبلت لتركها التتابع بلا ضرورة، بخلاف النفاس فإنّه أمر يقع أحياناً، فيقطع التتابع في صوم كلّ كفارة، وصوم ثلاثة أيام في كفارة اليمين يقطع تتابعه حيضها أيضاً. كذا في «البحر».
[2] قوله: أو بغيره؛ أي أفطر بغير العذر، وهذا تصريح بما علم منه سابقاً بالطريق الأولى، فإنّه لَمَّا كان الإفطار بعذر قاطعاً للتتابع كان الإفطار بغير عذر قاطعاً بالطريق الأولى.
[3] قوله: أو وطئها؛ أي التي ظاهر منها في أثناء الشهرين، فإن جامع غيرها فيهما، فإن كان وطءاً يفسد الصوم كالجماع بالنهار عامداً قطع التتابع، ويلزمه الاستئناف اتفاقاً، وإن لم يكن مفسداً بأن وطئها بالنهار ناسياً أو بالليل كيفما كان لم يقطع التتابع، فلا يلزمه الاستئناف اتفاقاً. كذا في «النهاية».
[4] قوله: عمداً؛ هذا القيد اتفاقي، فإنَّ الوطء بالليل عمداً ونسياناً سواء، وتقييد الوطء بالنهار بالنسيان؛ لأنه إذا جامعها فيه عامداً يستأنف بالاتفاق. كذا في «العناية».
[0] قوله: لا الإطعام؛ يعني لو قَرُبَ التي ظاهر منها في أثناء إطعام ستين مسكيناً.
[هذا عند أبي حنيفة ومحمد، وعند أبي يوسف له لا يستأنفُ الصَّوم؛ لأنَّه يجب أن يكون متتابعاً مقدماً على المسيس، فالتابع حاصل، بقي أن التقدم على المسيس غيرُ حاصل، لكنه إن استأنف يكون الكل مؤخَّراً عن المسيس، ولو لم يستأنف فبعضه مقدم على المسيس، فهذا أولى.
إن اختار الإطعام في الكفارة بعجزه عن العتق والصيام، لا يجب عليه استئناف الإطعام؛ لأنه لم يشترط في الإطعام أن يكون قبل المسيس، ولم يقل فيه: من قبل أن يتماسا كما قاله في التحرير والصيام، نعم يمنع عن الوطء قبل الإطعام منع تحريم لجواز قدرته على العتق والصيام، فيقعان بعد الوطء. كذا في «الهداية» وغيرها. وذكر في «الفتح» و «النهر»: إنّ القدرة حال قيام العجز بالفقر أو الكبر أو المرض الذي لا يرجى زواله أمر موهوم باعتبار الأمر الموهوم لا يثبت تحريم الوطء بل الاستحباب.
[1] قوله: وعند أبي يوسف ... الخ؛ ظاهره يقتضي كون خلافه في جميع الصور المذكورة وليس كذلك، فقد صرّح في «العناية» وغيرها أنَّ الخلاف في وطء لا يفسد الصوم، وهو الوطء في النهار سهوا، ويؤيده قول صاحب «الهداية) في تعليل مذهبه؛ لأنه لا يمنع التتابع؛ إذ لا يفسد به الصوم، وهو الشرط.
[2] قوله: لأنه يجب ... الخ؛ حاصله: أنّ الشرط هو كون الصوم متتابعاً من قبل أن يتماسًا، ففيما إذا صام متتابعاً ووطئ بالنهار سهواً، التتابع حاصل؛ لأنه لم يفطر عمداً، ولم يبطل صومه بوطئه نسياناً، وأما التقدّم على المسيس وإن بطل لكن في استئناف الصوم يكون جميعه مؤخّراً عن المسيس، ولو لم يستأنف يكون البعض مقدماً وبعضه مؤخراً، فاختيار هذا أولى من اختيار الاستئناف.
Book of Divorce and Zihar.
And if he breaks the fast with an excuse, or without it, or has intercourse with her during the two months at night intentionally, or by day forgetfully, he resumes the fast, not the feeding, if he had intercourse during it.
[1] His saying: with an excuse; like travel or illness or her postpartum bleeding in the expiation for accidental killing, and expiation for breaking the fast of Ramadan. As for her menstruation, it does not break the continuity in her fast for her killing her fast; because she cannot find two months free from it.
And she must connect what is after menstruation with what was before it. So if she breaks the fast after it by one day, she starts anew due to leaving continuity without necessity, unlike postpartum bleeding which occurs occasionally, so it breaks the continuity in the fast of every expiation. And the fast of three days in the expiation of an oath, her menstruation also breaks its continuity. Like this in al-Bahr.
[2] His saying: or without it; that is, he broke the fast without excuse. This is an explicit statement of what was known from it previously by a fortiori reasoning, for since breaking the fast with an excuse is cutting continuity, breaking the fast without an excuse is cutting by a fortiori reasoning.
[3] His saying: or has intercourse with her; that is, the one from whom he made zihar during the two months. If he has intercourse with another during them, then if it is intercourse that spoils the fast like intercourse by day intentionally, it cuts continuity, and he must resume by agreement. And if it does not spoil, such as if he has intercourse with her by day forgetfully or by night in any way, it does not cut continuity, and he does not have to resume by agreement. Like this in al-Nihaya.
[4] His saying: intentionally; this restriction is agreed upon, for intercourse by night intentionally and forgetfully are the same, and restricting intercourse by day to forgetfulness; because if he has intercourse with her during the day intentionally, he resumes by agreement. Like this in al-Inaya.
[0] His saying: not the feeding; that is, if he approaches the one from whom he made zihar during feeding sixty poor people.
[This is according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad. And according to Abu Yusuf, he does not have to resume the fast; because it must be continuous and preceding the contact. So the continuity is attained, but the precedence over contact is not attained. However, if he resumes, all of it is after contact, and if he does not resume, some of it is before contact and some after, so this is better.
If he chooses feeding in the expiation due to inability to free a slave and fast, he does not have to resume the feeding; because it was not stipulated in feeding that it be before contact, and he did not say in it: 'before they touch each other' as he said in the case of freeing and fasting. Yes, he is prohibited from intercourse before feeding as a prohibition of prohibition due to the possibility of his ability to free and fast, so they occur after intercourse. Like this in al-Hidaya and others. And it is mentioned in al-Fath and al-Nahr that ability while unable due to poverty or old age or an incurable illness is a fanciful notion; based on a fanciful notion, prohibition of intercourse is not established, rather recommended.
[1] His saying: And according to Abu Yusuf... etc.; its apparent meaning suggests that his disagreement is in all the mentioned images, but it is not so. For it is explicitly stated in al-Inaya and others that the disagreement is concerning intercourse that does not spoil the fast, and that is intercourse by day forgetfully. This is supported by the statement of the author of al-Hidaya in the reasoning of his school: because it does not prevent continuity, since the fast is not spoiled by it, and that is the condition.
[2] His saying: because it must... etc.; its gist: that the condition is that the fast be continuous from before they touch. So in the case where he fasts continuously and has intercourse by day forgetfully, continuity is attained because he did not break the fast intentionally, and his fast was not invalidated by his forgetful intercourse. As for precedence over contact, even if it is lost, but in resuming the fast all of it becomes after contact, while if he does not resume, some of it is before and some after, so choosing this is better than choosing resumption.
Source pages p. 421

Commentary
This passage deals with the intra-Hanafi dispute (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad vs. Abu Yusuf) regarding the effect of intercourse during the two-month fast of zihar expiation. The main text states that if a person breaks the fast (with or without excuse) or has intercourse during the two months—intentionally at night or forgetfully by day—he must resume the fast but does not need to resume the feeding if he had chosen that option. The commentary clarifies that breaking the fast even with an excuse (travel, illness) breaks the continuity of the fast, obligating a restart. It also distinguishes between menstruation and postpartum bleeding: menstruation does not break continuity in certain expiations, while postpartum bleeding does. The key dispute is between Abu Hanifa and Muhammad (who require resumption of the fast if intercourse occurs, even forgetfully by day) and Abu Yusuf (who does not require resumption if the fast remains valid because it was not intentionally broken). Abu Yusuf's reasoning is that the condition for the fast is that it be continuous and precede intercourse. If the fast is continuous (not broken intentionally), and intercourse occurs forgetfully by day, then despite losing precedence, not resuming means some fasting is before intercourse—better than all after. The passage also discusses the feeding option: if unable to fast, one may feed sixty poor people; feeding does not have the condition of being before intercourse, so no restart is needed. However, the prohibition of intercourse before feeding is merely recommended (not mandatory) because the inability may be temporary.

Key Terms
تتابع (tataabu'): The requirement of consecutive, unbroken days of fasting in expiation.
استئناف (isti'naf): Resuming or restarting the fast from the beginning after an interruption.
مسيس (masīs): Sexual intercourse (metaphorically 'touching').

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, what is the ruling if a person having made zihar has intercourse with his wife during the two-month fast by day forgetfully?
A) He must restart the fast because the intercourse, even if forgetful, breaks the condition of precedence.
B) He does not need to restart because the fast is still valid and continuous.
C) He must switch to the feeding option immediately.
D) He must repeat only the day of intercourse.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, the relied-upon position is that any intercourse during the two-month zihar fast, even forgetfully, requires restarting the fast to fulfill the condition of precedence.

When Accidental Intercourse Occurs During Kaffarah Fasting: The Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on restarting kaffarah fast after accidental intercourse

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the reason for the intra-Hanafi disagreement on restarting the kaffarah fast after accidental intercourse and state the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
Accidental intercourse during kaffarah fasting: must restart? Abu Hanifa and Muhammad say yes; Abu Yusuf says no.

This lesson examines a specific intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the two-month consecutive fast required as kaffarah for zihar. The point of contention is whether a person who forgetfully has intercourse during the daytime must restart the entire fast. The disagreement hinges on how strictly the Qur'anic condition 'before they touch each other' (min qabli an yatamassa) is interpreted.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Qur'anic condition for the kaffarah fast
The verse requires that the fast be both consecutive (mutatābi‘) and completed before marital relations (min qabli an yatamassa). Understand that these are two distinct conditions.
The condition is from Qur'an 58:3-4.
2
Apply the condition to the scenario of accidental intercourse
A person fasting for kaffarah accidentally has intercourse during the day. The fast itself is not invalidated (since it was forgetful), but the condition of being entirely before intercourse is compromised.
Accidental intercourse does not break the fast according to the Hanafi school.
3
Compare the reasoning of Abu Hanifa/Muhammad vs. Abu Yusuf
Abu Hanifa and Muhammad argue that the entire fast must be free from intercourse, so restarting is necessary. Abu Yusuf argues that part of the fast being before intercourse is better than none, so no restart is needed.
Abu Yusuf's view applies only when the fast is not invalidated (i.e., forgetful intercourse).
4
Determine the mufta bihi position and its rationale
The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad: restart the fast. This is the cautious approach that fully satisfies the Qur'anic condition.
The tarjih is made by later Hanafi authorities like al-Marghinani.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هذا عند أبي حنيفة ومحمد ، وعند أبي يوسف له لا يستأنفُ الصَّوم ؛ لأنَّه يجب أن يكون متتابعاً مقدماً على المسيس، فالتابع حاصل، بقي أن التقدم على المسيس غيرُ حاصل ، لكنه إن استأنف يكون الكل مؤخَّراً عن المسيس، ولو لم يستأنف فبعضه مقدم على المسيس، فهذا أولى. [قوله : وعند أبي يوسف ... الخ ؛ ظاهره يقتضي كون خلافه في جميع الصور المذكورة وليس كذلك ، فقد صرّح في «العناية» وغيرها أنَّ الخلاف في وطء لا يفسد الصوم، وهو الوطء في النهار سهوا ، ويؤيده قول صاحب «الهداية» في تعليل مذهبه ؛ لأنه لا يمنع التتابع ؛ إذ لا يفسد به الصوم، وهو الشرط. [قوله : لأنه يجب ... الخ ؛ حاصله : أنّ الشرط هو كون الصوم متتابعاً من قبل أن يتماسًا، ففيما إذا صام متتابعاً ووطئ بالنهار سهواً، التتابع حاصل ؛ لأنه لم يفطر عمداً، ولم يبطل صومه بوطئه نسياناً ، وأما التقدّم على المسيس وإن بطل لكن في استئناف الصوم يكون جميعه مؤخّراً عن المسيس، ولو لم يستأنف يكون البعض مقدماً وبعضه مؤخراً، فاختيار هذا أولى من اختيار الاستئناف. وكذلك لأبي حنيفة ومحمد أنه يجب أن يكون مقدماً على المسيس خالياً عنه ، فالتقدم على المسيس قد فات، لكن خُلُوهُ عن المسيس ممكن ، فتجب رعايته.
This is according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad. According to Abu Yusuf, he does not have to restart the fast because the fast must be consecutive and precede sexual intercourse. Consecutiveness is achieved; what remains is that precedence over intercourse is not achieved. But if he restarts, the entire fast would be after intercourse; if he does not restart, part of it would be before intercourse. This is preferable. [Comment: 'And according to Abu Yusuf...' apparently implies that his disagreement applies to all mentioned cases, but it is not so. Indeed, it is explicitly stated in al-'Inayah and others that the disagreement is regarding intercourse that does not invalidate the fast, which is intercourse during the day forgetfully. This is supported by the statement of the author of al-Hidayah in explaining his madhhab: because it does not prevent consecutiveness since the fast is not invalidated by it, and that is the condition. [Comment: 'Because it must...' its summary: The condition is that the fast be consecutive and before intercourse. In the case where he fasts consecutively and then has intercourse during the day forgetfully, consecutiveness is achieved because he did not break the fast intentionally, and his fast is not invalidated by his forgetful intercourse. As for precedence over intercourse, although it is lost, if he restarts the fast, the entire fast would be after intercourse; if he does not restart, part of it would be before and part after. Choosing this is better than choosing restart. And similarly, according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, it must be before intercourse and free from it. Precedence over intercourse is lost, but freedom from intercourse is possible, so it must be observed.
Source pages p. 422

Commentary
This passage addresses the ruling when a person performing the two-month consecutive fast as kaffarah for zihar accidentally has intercourse during the daytime. The Hanafi school has an intra-madhhab disagreement. According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani, the person must restart the entire fast because the condition of the fast being both consecutive and before intercourse is not fully met. The fast was consecutive, but it was not wholly before intercourse due to the interruption. However, according to Abu Yusuf, restarting is not required. His reasoning is that the condition of consecutiveness is satisfied since the fast was not broken intentionally. As for the requirement of being before intercourse, not restarting is better because then at least part of the fast is before intercourse, whereas restarting would make the entire fast after intercourse. The commentator clarifies that Abu Yusuf's position applies only when the intercourse does not invalidate the fast, i.e., when it occurs forgetfully. If the fast were invalidated, the disagreement would not apply. The condition of the fast being before intercourse and free from it is further explained: precedence over intercourse is lost, but freedom from intercourse is still possible if he restarts and avoids intercourse. Therefore, Abu Hanifa and Muhammad require restarting to ensure the fast is completely free from intercourse.

Key Terms
التتابع (al-tatābu‘): Consecutiveness of fasting days without interruption.
المسيس (al-masīs): Sexual intercourse, specifically the act that is the subject of the kaffarah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Continue the fast without change.
B) Restart the entire two-month fast.
C) Feed sixty poor people instead.
D) Make up only that day's fast.

Key Takeaway
In kaffarah fasting, the condition of the fast being entirely before intercourse is strictly upheld by Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, requiring restart if any intercourse occurs.

Agency and Payment of Value in Kaffarah Feeding
Rulings on agency and cash value in kaffarah feeding with proof from qiyas.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on agency and payment of cash value in expiatory feeding, and explain the evidence and differences with Imam al-Shafi'i.

Core Idea
Hanafi school permits delegation and cash value in feeding expiations, unlike Shafi'i's literal requirement.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah explains the flexibility within the Hanafi school for fulfilling the feeding obligation in kaffarah. When a person cannot fast for zihar, he must feed sixty poor people. The commentator clarifies that this can be done by a deputy (agent) because it is a financial act of worship, and furthermore, the cash value of the food may be given instead of the food itself. These rulings are supported by analogy (qiyas) from a specified expiation in hadith. The passage also notes the intra-Hanafi difference on agency in freeing a slave and the inter-madhhab dispute with al-Shafi'i on cash value.

Guided Path
1
Identify the obligation and alternatives
The kaffarah for zihar involves freeing a slave, fasting two consecutive months, or feeding sixty poor people. This passage addresses the feeding option when the person is unable to fast.
The feeding is wajib in this scenario.
2
Recognize that feeding is a financial worship allowing agency
Feeding is classified as 'ibadah maliyah (financial worship), so the person may appoint a deputy (na'ib) to perform it on his behalf. This is agreed upon.
Contrast with freeing a slave where Abu Yusuf allows agency but Abu Hanifa and Muhammad do not.
3
Understand the measure of food per poor person
Each poor person receives the amount of zakat al-fitr: half a sa' of wheat or a sa' of dates/barley. This is derived by qiyas from the expiation for a muhrim shaving his head, as recorded in Sahih collections.
The hadith specifies feeding six poor people with a sa' of dates; analogy extends this measure to all expiations.
4
Analyze the permissibility of paying cash value
The Hanafi school allows giving the cash value (qimah) instead of the actual food. The reasoning is that value is equivalent in meaning (mithl) and may be more beneficial to the poor.
This is based on the principle that value can substitute for the commodity in financial obligations.
5
Examine the inter-madhhab dispute
Imam al-Shafi'i does not permit cash value because the texts specify food, and acts of worship are tawqifiyyah (cannot be extended by analogy). The Hanafi view relies on qiyas and maslahah.
The practical result: Hanafis can pay cash; Shafi'is must give food.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن عجز عن الصوم أطعم هو أو نائبه ستين مسكيناً كلا قدر الفِطْرَة، أو قيمته. [قوله : هو أو نائبه ؛ يشير إلى أن الإطعام عبادة مالية، فتجري فيه النيابة والوكالة، وكذا في تحرير رقبة عند أبي يوسف، خلافا لهما. [قوله : كلا ؛ أي يعطي كلّ مسكين بقدر الفطرة ؛ أي صدقة الفطر، وهي نصف صاع من بر أو صاع من تمر أو شعير، على ما مر تفصيله في موضعه، وهذا هو التقدير عندنا في الكفارات، ردّاً لغير المنصوص إلى المنصوص، فقد ورد في رواية جميع أصحاب الصحاح التصريح به في كفارة حلق المحرم رأسه، فكذا في غيره. [قوله : أو قيمته ؛ أي يجوز أن يؤدّي قيمته قدر الفطرة، وهذا عندنا في جميع المقادير والكفارات المنصوصة، بناءً على أنّ قيمة الشيء مثل له معنى، وقد تكون أنفع للفقير، فيجوز دفعها. (وإن غداهم) [قوله : وإن غداهم؛ - بتشديد الدال، وكذا قرينه بتشديد الشين، من التغدية. [وأما عند الشافعي لا يجوز دفع القيمة].
And if he is unable to fast, he or his deputy feeds sixty poor people, each with the amount of fitranah (zakat al-fitr), or its value. [Comment: 'He or his deputy' indicates that feeding is a financial act of worship, so agency and deputyship apply, similarly in freeing a slave according to Abu Yusuf, contrary to the two (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad). [Comment: 'Each' means he gives each poor person the amount of fitranah, i.e., zakat al-fitr, which is half a sa' of wheat or a sa' of dates or barley, as detailed elsewhere. This is the measurement in our school for expiations, analogizing unspecified cases to specified ones. It is reported in the authentic collections regarding the expiation for a muhrim shaving his head, so similarly for others. [Comment: 'Or its value' means it is permissible to give its value equivalent to the fitranah amount. This is our view regarding all specified measures and expiations, based on the principle that the value of a thing is like its substance in meaning, and may be more beneficial for the poor, so it is permissible to give it. (And if he feeds them lunch...) [Comment: 'And if he feeds them lunch' with a shaddah on the dal, and similarly its counterpart with shaddah on the shin, from al-taghdiya. [And according to al-Shafi'i, paying the value is not permissible.]
Source pages p. 422

Commentary
This passage discusses the feeding option in the kaffarah for zihar when the person is unable to fast. The text states that the person or his deputy may feed sixty poor people, each receiving the amount of zakat al-fitr (fitranah), or the cash equivalent. The commentator explains several points: First, agency is permitted because feeding is a financial act of worship (ibadah maliyah). This applies also to freeing a slave according to Abu Yusuf, while Abu Hanifa and Muhammad do not allow agency in freeing a slave. Second, the amount per poor person is defined as half a sa' of wheat or a sa' of dates or barley, based on analogy (qiyas) from the expiation for a muhrim shaving his head, which is specified in hadith. This measurement is used for all expiations in the Hanafi school. Third, paying the value (qimah) instead of the food itself is permissible in the Hanafi school because the value is considered equivalent in meaning and may be more beneficial to the poor. This is based on the principle that value can substitute for the commodity. The commentator notes that Imam al-Shafi'i disagrees and does not permit paying the value; the Hanafi view allows it. The phrase 'and if he feeds them lunch' indicates an alternative method of feeding, i.e., providing a meal instead of giving the raw food, but the commentary does not elaborate further on the Shafi'i view here.

Key Terms
الفطرة (al-fiṭrah): Zakat al-fitr, the charity given at the end of Ramadan; used here as the standard measure of food for kaffarah feeding.
القيمة (al-qīmah): Cash value of the food items specified for kaffarah feeding.
النيابة (al-niyābah): Deputyship/agency in performing the act of feeding, permissible because it is a financial act.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Explicit Quranic verse permitting cash
B) Analogy from the expiation for shaving the head in ihram
C) Consensus of the Companions
D) Custom of the people of Medina

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, feeding sixty poor people for kaffarah can be done by an agent and with cash value, based on qiyas and the financial nature of the act.

Feeding Sixty Poor for Multiple Expiation Obligations
Ruling on feeding sixty poor for multiple expiations with intra-Hanafi dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on feeding sixty poor for multiple expiations of the same type versus different types, and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa/Abu Yusuf and Muhammad.

Core Idea
Feeding sixty poor for two zihars only expiates one; but for a zihar and a broken fast, it expiates both — intention operates only when types differ.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya presents a nuanced ruling on whether feeding sixty poor people once can discharge two expiation obligations. The ruling depends on whether the obligations are of the same type (e.g., two zihars) or different types (e.g., zihar and broken fast). An intra-Hanafi dispute exists between Abu Hanifa/Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad on the role of intention in distinguishing identical expiations. Let us unpack the reasoning and practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
State the default ruling
Identify the ruling in the matn: feeding sixty poor for two zihars discharges only one zihar; for a zihar and a broken fast, both are discharged. Note that this is the position of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf.
The matn explicitly states the ruling without mentioning the disagreement initially.
2
Identify the intra-Hanafi dispute
Recognize that Imam Muhammad disagrees with the two. He holds that if the person intends to expiate both same-type obligations, the feeding discharges both.
The disagreement is based on whether intention can separate two identical acts.
3
Analyze the reasoning of each position
For the two: Intention is meaningless when the obligations are identical; the sa' given to each poor person suffices for only one expiation. For Muhammad: The sa' can be divided (half for each expiation) and the poor person is the locus for both.
The key principle: intention operates only when types differ (ikhtilaf al-jins).
4
Determine the practical consequence
Thamarah al-khilaf: According to the relied-upon position (the two), the person must perform a second feeding for the second expiation. According to Muhammad, one feeding suffices.
The passage also notes that this applies only when obligations are of the same type.
