وإن صَلَحَ هو شاهداً، وهي أمةٌ، أو كافرة، أو محدودة في قذف، أو صبية، أو مجنونة، أو زانية فلا حد عليه، ولا لعان؛ لأنها إن اتَّصفَتْ بالزنا لا تكون عفيفة. والأصل فيه على ما في «البحر» و«المنح» وغيرهما: إنَّ اللعان إذا سقط لمعنى من جهة بأن لم يصلح شاهداً لرقّه أو كفره، فلو كان القذفُ صحيحاً حد وإلا فلا حد ولا لعان. وأمّا لو سقط لمعنى من جهتهما فلا حد ولا لعان، ولو سقط من جهتهما كما لو كانا محدودين في قذف فهو كالأول. وصورته: أن يقول هو أولاً أربع مرات: أشهد بالله أني صادق فيما رميتها به من الزنا، وفي الخامسة: لعنة الله عليه إن كان كاذباً فيما رماها به من الزنا. فلا لعان؛ لعدم أهلية الشهادة، ولا حد؛ لأنه سقط اللعان لمعنى من جهتها لا من جهته. بغيره: أي بغير الزنا، وهو الجنون والصغر والرق والكفر والحد في قذف. ولا لعان؛ يدلّ عليه حديث: أربعة من النساء لا ملاعنة بينهن: النصرانية تحت المسلم، واليهودية تحت المسلم، والمملوكة تحت الحر، والحرّة تحت المملوك، أخرجه ابن ماجة وغيره.
And if he is fit as a witness, and she is a female slave, or a disbeliever, or one who has been flogged for slander, or a minor, or insane, or an adulteress, then there is no prescribed punishment upon him and no mutual imprecation, because if she is characterized by adultery she is not chaste. And the principle in this, as in 'Al-Bahr' and 'Al-Munah' and others: if the mutual imprecation falls due to a reason on [her] side, such as her not being fit as a witness due to slavery or disbelief, then if the slander was valid, there is a prescribed punishment; otherwise there is no punishment and no mutual imprecation. But if it falls due to a reason on both sides, there is no punishment and no mutual imprecation; and if it falls from both sides, as if both are flogged for slander, it is like the first. And its description: that he says first four times: 'I bear witness by Allah that I am truthful in what I accused her of from adultery,' and in the fifth: 'The curse of Allah be upon him if he is lying in what he accused her of from adultery.' So there is no mutual imprecation due to lack of eligibility for testimony, and no prescribed punishment because the mutual imprecation fell due to a reason from her side not from his. 'Or other than it': meaning other than adultery - it is insanity, minority, slavery, disbelief, and punishment for slander. And no mutual imprecation is indicated by the hadith: 'Four types of women there is no mutual imprecation between: a Christian woman under a Muslim, a Jewish woman under a Muslim, a female slave under a free man, and a free woman under a slave' - related by Ibn Majah and others.
Source pages p. 434

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof concerning the conditions for li'an (mutual imprecation) and hadd (punishment for false accusation). The author, from the Hanafi school, states that if the husband is a valid witness but the wife is not (due to slavery, disbelief, being a former slanderer, minority, insanity, or being an adulteress), then there is no hadd on the husband and no li'an. The reason is that li'an requires the woman to be 'afifah (chaste) and eligible for testimony; if she is known for adultery or lacks qualifications, she is not 'afifah. The commentary expands: The principle is that if li'an falls due to a reason related to the wife (like her slavery or kufr preventing testimony), then if the husband's accusation was valid (i.e., he had intended it as a slander, but the woman was not actually chaste), he is not punished. If the accusation was false and she was chaste, he would be punished for qadhf. If li'an falls due to a reason related to both (both are former slanderers, etc.), then no hadd and no li'an. The passage also gives the description (surah) of li'an: the husband says four times 'I swear by Allah I am truthful in my accusation' and the fifth time 'curse of Allah upon me if I am lying'. This is the Hanafi method. Then it cites a hadith from Ibn Majah listing four women with whom there is no li'an: a Christian or Jewish wife under a Muslim husband, a slave woman under a free man, and a free woman under a slave. This hadith serves as the proof for the ruling.

Key Terms
لعان (li'an): A procedure of mutual imprecation between spouses when husband accuses wife of zina without witnesses, consisting of oaths and curses.
إحصان (ihsan): In context of li'an: the state of being a free, adult, sane, Muslim, and chaste person.
شهادة (shahadah): Testimony; eligibility to be a witness in court.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The husband undergoes li'an and is free from hadd
B) The husband is flogged for qadhf
C) There is no li'an and no hadd
D) The wife is stoned

Key Takeaway
Li'an is only valid when both spouses are eligible for testimony; otherwise, no hadd and no li'an.

The Form (Surah) of Li'an in the Hanafi School
Ruling statement: exact wording of li'an

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to recite the exact wording of the husband's li'an and identify the number and content of each utterance.

Core Idea
The husband's li'an consists of four testimonies and one curse — the fifth utterance is a la'nah, not a shahada.

The Quranic procedure of li'an (mutual cursing) is prescribed when a husband accuses his wife of adultery without four witnesses. The Hanafi school specifies the exact wording the husband must pronounce. This lesson details the surah (form) of li'an as outlined in classical Hanafi texts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the setting
The li'an takes place in the presence of the judge, with the wife present in the court session.
The wife's presence is necessary for the pointing.
2
Recite the first four utterances
Each of the first four utterances begins with 'Ashhadu billah...' (I bear witness by Allah) affirming that the husband is truthful in his accusation of adultery.
The husband must repeat this exact phrase four times.
3
Point to the wife
After each utterance, the husband points to his wife, and may address her directly using 'what I accused you of' instead of 'what I accused her of'.
Pointing is a procedural requirement; addressing her directly is optional.
4
Deliver the fifth utterance
The fifth utterance is a curse: 'La'natullahi alayhi in kana kaziban...' (The curse of Allah be upon him if he is lying). This is not a testimony but an invocation of Allah's curse upon himself.
This distinction is crucial: the fifth is not a shahada.
5
Understand the consequence
After the husband completes his five utterances, the wife responds with a similar set (four shahadat that he is lying, and a fifth invoking Allah's wrath). This completes the li'an, resulting in irrevocable separation and avoidance of hadd for the husband.
The wife's form is not detailed in this passage but follows the same pattern.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وصورته: أن يقول هو أولاً أربع مرات: أشهد بالله أني صادق فيما رميتها به من الزنا، وفي الخامسة: لعنة الله عليه إن كان كاذباً فيما رماها به من الزنا. ويشير في كل مرة إلى الزوجة الحاضرة في مجلس القضاء، وإن شاء قال مخاطباً: فيما رميتك به.
And its description: that he says first four times: 'I bear witness by Allah that I am truthful in what I accused her of from adultery,' and in the fifth: 'The curse of Allah be upon him if he is lying in what he accused her of from adultery.' And he points each time to the wife present in the court session, and if he wishes he may address her: 'in what I accused you of.'
Source pages p. 435

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement describing the exact wording (surah) of li'an according to the Hanafi school. The husband begins by saying four times: 'I bear witness by Allah that I am truthful in my accusation of adultery against her (or you).' In the fifth statement, he says: 'The curse of Allah be upon me if I am lying in my accusation.' The text adds a procedural detail: the husband should point to his wife present in the court, and he may address her directly. This form is based on the Quranic verse in Surah An-Nur (24:6-9). The Hanafi position is that the husband's li'an consists of five utterances: four testimonies (shahadat) and one curse (la'nah). The wife then responds with similar five utterances: four times 'I bear witness by Allah that he is lying' and the fifth 'The wrath of Allah be upon me if he is truthful'. This completes the li'an, resulting in the irrevocable separation of the couple and avoidance of hadd for the husband.

Key Terms
صورة (surah): The form or manner in which the li'an is performed, i.e., the prescribed wording.
أشهد بالله (ashhadu billah): I bear witness by Allah; a formula for swearing testimony.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway

The Procedure and Legal Effect of Li'an
Describes the form of li'an and the dispute over judicial separation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the exact wording of the li'an oaths and explain why the Hanafi school requires a judicial decree for separation, identifying the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf with Zufar.

Core Idea
Li'an oaths do not automatically separate the couple; the judge must decree separation.

Li'an is a solemn procedure when a husband accuses his wife of adultery without four witnesses. The exchange of oaths is detailed, but the key legal dispute within the Hanafi school is whether these oaths themselves end the marriage or whether a judicial decree is required. This lesson unpacks the procedure and the proof for the relied-upon position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the procedure of li'an
Read the Arabic passage and note the exact wording of the husband's five statements and the wife's five statements. Understand that the husband's fifth invokes a curse on himself, while the wife's fifth invokes wrath on herself.
The text distinguishes between the husband's 'curse' (لعنة) and the wife's 'wrath' (غضب) for psychological deterrence.
2
Locate the disputed point
After the oaths, the text states 'then they are separated.' The commentary explains that some scholars (Zufar) held that separation occurs automatically upon completing li'an, but the majority requires a judge's decree.
3
Examine the majority's proof
The majority cites the hadith of 'Uwaymir: after li'an, he said to the Prophet 'I would have lied about her if I kept her' and then divorced her. This shows that the marriage was still intact after li'an, otherwise divorce would be meaningless.
4
Examine Zufar's proof
Zufar uses the hadith 'the two li'aners never reunite' from al-Daraqutni and al-Bayhaqi. The majority responds that this means after the judge separates them, supported by Abu Dawud's version: 'the sunna is that they are separated and then never reunite.'
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: according to the majority, if one spouse dies after li'an but before the judge's decree, inheritance occurs. Zufar holds immediate separation, so no inheritance. The mufta bihi is the majority view.

In the fifth: 'May the curse of Allah be upon him if he is lying in his accusation of her of adultery.' ... The wife then says four times: 'I bear witness by Allah that he is lying in his accusation of me of adultery.' In the fifth: 'May the wrath of Allah be upon her if he is truthful in his accusation of me of adultery.' Then they are separated. The commentary explains that the judge must separate them, contrary to Zufar who held that li'an itself causes separation. Source pages p. 435

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on al-Wiqayah describes the procedure of li'an (mutual imprecation) when a husband accuses his wife of adultery without witnesses. The husband must make five statements: four declarations of truthfulness and a fifth invoking curse upon himself if lying. The wife then makes five similar statements, with the fifth invoking wrath upon herself if her husband is truthful. The text then addresses a controversy among later Hanafis: Imam Zufar held that li'an itself automatically effects permanent separation. However, the relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that the judge must decree the separation after li'an is complete. Proof is from the incident of 'Uwaymir where the Prophet separated them after li'an. If separation is not decreed, inheritance and other marital effects persist until the judge's ruling. This distinction has practical consequences for when talaq and inheritance apply.

Key Terms
اللعان (al-li'an): Mutual imprecation between spouses when the husband accuses his wife of adultery without four witnesses; the process of exchanging oaths that results in separation.
التفريق (al-tafriq): The judicial decree separating the li'an couple; it is a legal act by the qadi, not an automatic consequence.
قذف (qadhf): The act of accusing a married person of adultery; false qadhf incurs the hadd punishment (80 lashes).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Li'an oaths alone do not end the marriage; a judicial decree is required, and this has practical consequences for inheritance and divorce.

Judicial Separation Required After Li'an
Commentary on ruling that judge must separate after li'an

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school requires a judge's decree for separation after li'an and evaluate Zufar's counter-argument using textual evidence.

Core Idea
Separation after li'an requires a judge's decree; the marriage remains intact until the judge separates.

In the case of li'an (mutual cursing), a common assumption is that the marriage ends immediately when the oaths are concluded. However, the Hanafi school, citing the Prophet's practice with 'Uwaymir al-'Ajlani, holds that separation is not automatic. This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah explains the reasoning and responds to an opposing view within the school itself.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Hanafi ruling
The Hanafi school states that the judge must separate after li'an; li'an itself does not separate.
The text says 'yajib 'ala al-qadi an yufarriq baynahuma'.
2
Understand the opposing view
Zufar held that separation occurs automatically upon completion of li'an, based on a hadith.
Zufar is a major Hanafi scholar but his view is marjuh.
3
Examine Zufar's proof
Zufar relied on the hadith 'al-mutala'inan la yajtami'an abadan' (they never reunite).
Recorded by al-Daraqutni and al-Bayhaqi.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafis respond that the hadith refers to after the judge's separation, citing a version in Sunan Abi Dawud: 'maddat al-sunnah fi al-mutala'inan an yufraq baynahuma thumma la yajtami'an.'
Also, 'Uwaymir's divorce after li'an shows the marriage was intact.
5
Determine the practical difference
If separation were automatic, inheritance and divorce would not apply after li'an before judicial separation; the Hanafi ruling preserves these.
Thamarah al-khilaf: inheritance and post-li'an divorce are valid only if marriage remains.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ثم يفرق ؛ أي بعدما تلاعنا ، يجب على القاضي الذي تلاعنا عنده أن يفرق بينهما ، لما روى أن النبي ﷺ فرق بين عويمر العجلاني وزوجته بعد تلاعنهما، أخرجه البخاري وغيره من أصحاب الصحاح. وأشار به إلى أن نفس التلاعن لا يثبت به التفريق، كما قال به زفره، بل لا بد من تفريق الحاكم، فيجري التوارث إن مات أحدهما بعد التلاعن قبل تفريقه، ويقع عليهما الطلاق إن طلق عند ذلك، وحجة زفر ظاهر حديث: «المتلاعنان لا يجتمعان أبدا»، أخرجه الدارقطني والبيهقي من حديث ابن عمر والجواب عنه : إن المراد بعد تفريق الحاكم، تشهد له ما في «سنن أبي داود: مضت ألسنة في المتلاعنين أن يفرق بينهما ثم لا يجتمعان»، والذي يدل على أن التفريق لا يقع بنفس التلاعن ما ورد في «صحيح البخاري» وغيره: «إن عويمر العجلاني الله بعد ما لا عن قال : يا رسول الله كذبت عليها إن أمسكتها، فطلقها ثلاثا»
He said: 'Then he separates' i.e., after they have performed li'an, the judge before whom they performed li'an must separate between them, because it is narrated that the Prophet ﷺ separated between 'Uwaymir al-'Ajlani and his wife after their li'an, recorded by al-Bukhari and others. And he indicated by this that li'an itself does not establish separation, as Zufar said, rather the judge's separation is indispensable. So inheritance takes effect if one of them dies after li'an before separation, and divorce applies if he divorces at that time. Zufar's evidence is the apparent meaning of the hadith: 'The two who perform li'an never reunite,' recorded by al-Daraqutni and al-Bayhaqi from Ibn 'Umar. The response is that it means after the judge's separation, supported by the version in Sunan Abi Dawud: 'The practice concerning the two who perform li'an is that they are separated, then they never reunite.' And what indicates that separation does not occur by li'an itself is what is in Sahih al-Bukhari and others: 'Indeed 'Uwaymir al-'Ajlani, after he performed li'an, said: O Messenger of Allah, I would have lied against her if I kept her, so he divorced her three times.'
Source pages p. 436

Commentary
This passage expands on the matn's statement 'then he separates.' The commentator clarifies that the separation is not automatic but must be executed by the judge. He supports this with the practice of the Prophet ﷺ in the case of 'Uwaymir al-'Ajlani. The school of Zufar is mentioned as holding that li'an itself effects separation based on a hadith 'al-mutala'inan la yajtami'an abadan.' The Hanafi response is that the hadith refers to after the judge's separation, as shown by the version in Sunan Abi Dawud. Further evidence that separation is not automatic comes from the incident where 'Uwaymir divorced his wife after li'an, indicating the marriage was still intact. The practical consequence: if li'an separated automatically, then inheritance would not apply if one dies after li'an but before judicial separation, and the husband could not divorce. The Hanafi ruling that the judge must separate is adopted.

Key Terms
التلاعن (al-li'an): The mutual cursing procedure between spouses when the husband accuses the wife of adultery and she denies it, involving repeated oaths.
التفريق (al-tafriq): The judicial decree of separation between the spouses after li'an.
زفر (Zufar): Zufar ibn al-Hudhayl, a major Hanafi scholar who sometimes held different opinions from Abu Hanifa.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, when does separation occur in li'an (mutual cursing)?
A) Immediately upon the completion of the li'an procedure
B) Only after the judge formally decrees separation
C) After the wife's fifth oath
D) After three menstrual cycles

Key Takeaway
Li'an does not automatically separate spouses; a judge must issue a separation decree for the marriage to dissolve.

If Judge Dies After Li'an: Repeating the Procedure
Intra-Hanafi dispute on repeating li'an if judge dies

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on repeating li'an when the original judge is unavailable and distinguish it from Imam Muhammad's view.

Core Idea
The li'an must be completed before the same judge who issues the separation; otherwise, the procedure must be repeated.

When a judge oversees the li'an procedure but dies or is removed before formally separating the couple, a new judge is appointed. This lesson examines an intra-Hanafi dispute: must the new judge repeat the entire li'an, or may he proceed directly to separation? The relied-upon view requires repetition, while Imam Muhammad held it unnecessary.

Guided Path
1
State the scenario
Identify the trigger: the judge who heard the li'an has died or been removed before issuing the separation decree.
2
Identify the relied-upon Hanafi position
The text says the second judge must repeat the li'an before separating. This is the view favored by the author, citing al-Ikhtiyar.
3
Recognize the opposing view
Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani held that repetition is unnecessary; the previous li'an suffices.
4
Analyze the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
If the li'an is not repeated, the separation may be considered invalid according to the dominant view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : القاضي ; فلو لم يفرق حتى عزل أو مات استأنف الحاكم الثاني اللعان، ثم يفرق خلافاً لمحمد رحمه الله، فإن قال : لا يحتاج إلى استئنافه. كذا في «الاختيار شرح المختار»
He said: 'The judge' – so if he did not separate until he was removed or died, the second judge repeats the li'an, then separates, contrary to Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on him), for he said: There is no need to repeat it. Thus in al-Ikhtiyar Sharh al-Mukhtar.
Source pages p. 437

Commentary
This passage clarifies the ruling if the judge dies or is removed after the couple has performed li'an but before he formally separates them. The relied-upon position is that the new judge must repeat the entire li'an procedure before issuing the separation. Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani, however, held that the previous li'an is sufficient and does not need to be repeated. The author cites al-Ikhtiyar as the source of this ruling. The practical difference is that if the li'an is not repeated, the validity of the separation may be questioned according to the dominant view.

Key Terms
استئناف (isti'naf): To start again or repeat the li'an procedure.
اللعان (al-li'an): The mutual cursing procedure between spouses in cases of accusation of adultery.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) Dismiss the case

Key Takeaway
The relied-upon Hanafi position requires repeating the entire li'an procedure if the original judge is unavailable before separation, contrary to Imam Muhammad's view.

The Four Judicial Consequences of Li'an
Rulings on effects of li'an: lineage, child, and wife becoming ba'in

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the four consequences of li'an and identify the condition under which lineage is not negated.

Core Idea
In li'an, the judge separates the couple, negates the child's lineage (only if alive), attaches the child to the mother, and the wife becomes ba'in with one talaq.

Li'an (mutual cursing) between spouses leads to a permanent separation. The Hanafi school outlines four specific actions the judge must take after the li'an process. This commentary from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya explains each ruling in detail, including an important exception regarding the negation of lineage.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four outcomes
Read the Arabic sentence: 'ثم يفرق القاضي، وينفي نسبه، ويُلحقه بأمه، وتبين بطلقة'. List the four consequences in order.
2
Understand the condition for negating lineage
The commentator clarifies that negation of lineage is not automatic. It requires the child to be alive at the time of li'an. If the child died before li'an, the judge still separates but does not negate lineage.
This exception is mentioned in 'al-Nihaya'.
3
Recognize the legal status of the wife
The wife becomes 'ba'in' (irrevocably divorced) with one talaq. This is a unique feature of li'an, as a single talaq normally results in a revocable divorce.
The purpose is to achieve complete severance and remove injustice.
4
Note the proof for attaching the child to the mother
The Hanafi ruling is based on the Prophet's action with Hilal ibn Umayyah, who accused his wife and denied paternity. The Prophet attached the child to the mother, setting a precedent.
The hadith is recorded by Abu Dawud.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم يفرق القاضي، وينفي نسبه، ويُلحقه بأمه، وتبين بطلقة
قوله : وينفي ; أي يصرح الحاكم بنفي نسب الولد عنه ، ويقول : قطعت نسب هذا الولد عنه، بعدما قال : فرقت بينكما، وليس من ضرورة التفريق نفي النسب، كما إذا وقع التلاعن بعد موت الولد، فإنه حينئذ يفرق بينهما، ولا ينتفي النسب. كذا في «النهاية».
قوله : ويلحقه ; من الإلحاق ; أي يلحق القاضي ذلك الولد بالأم على ما هو الحكم في أولاد الزنا، كذلك فعل رسول الله ﷺ في ولد هلال بن أمية، وكان قد قذف زوجته بالزنا، ونفى الولد، أخرجه أبو داود وغيره.
قوله : وتبين ; أي تصير المرأة بعد تفريق الحاكم بائنة بطلقة، وتكون الفرقة في حكم الطلاق البائن ; لأن المقصود دفع الظلم عنها، وحصول الانقطاع التام بينهما.
Then the judge separates, negates his lineage, attaches him to his mother, and she becomes ba'in with one talaq. He said: 'and negates' i.e., the judge explicitly negates the child's lineage from him, and says: I cut off the lineage of this child from him, after he says: I have separated between you. And it is not necessary that negation of lineage accompanies separation, as when li'an occurs after the child's death, then he separates but lineage is not negated. Thus in al-Nihaya. He said: 'and attaches' from attachment; i.e., the judge attaches that child to its mother according to the ruling for children of fornication. Similarly did the Messenger of Allah ﷺ with the child of Hilal ibn Umayyah, who had accused his wife of fornication and negated the child, recorded by Abu Dawud and others. He said: 'and she becomes ba'in' i.e., the woman after the judge's separation becomes ba'in (irrevocably separate) by one talaq, and the separation is in the ruling of ba'in divorce because the purpose is to remove injustice from her and achieve complete severance between them.
Source pages p. 437

Commentary
The commentator explains the four consequences of li'an: 1) The judge separates them. 2) He declares the child's lineage negated from the husband. 3) He attaches the child to the mother, likening it to a child of fornication. This is based on the Prophet's action with Hilal ibn Umayyah. 4) The wife becomes ba'in (irrevocably divorced) by one talaq, meaning the marriage ends permanently. The commentary also clarifies that negation of lineage is not automatic; if the li'an occurs after the child's death, the judge still separates but does not negate lineage. The wife's ba'in status ensures complete severance, protecting her from injustice.

Key Terms
نفي النسب (nafy al-nasab): The judge's declaration that the child is not the husband's legitimate offspring.
بائنة (ba'inah): A woman who is irrevocably divorced, meaning the marriage ends without the right of return except through a new marriage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a li'an case, when does the judge NOT negate the child's lineage?
A) When the husband accuses without denying paternity
B) When the child has died before the li'an
C) When the wife confesses to adultery
D) When the judge forgets to mention it

Key Takeaway
The judge must perform four acts after li'an: separate, negate lineage (if child alive), attach child to mother, and make wife ba'in with one talaq.

Hadd for False Accusation After Li'an and Permissibility of Remarriage
Ruling on consequences of false admission after li'an

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the hadd punishment and remarriage when a husband admits lying after li'an.

Core Idea
Admission of false accusation after li'an triggers hadd but permits remarriage.

In marriage jurisprudence, li'an is a serious procedure where a husband accuses his wife of adultery without proof. If the husband later admits he was lying, the law imposes the hadd punishment for false accusation (qadhf) and allows the couple to remarry. This lesson explains the ruling and its rationale.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act
The husband, after li'an, admits his accusation was false.
2
Determine the hukm for the husband
The hadd of eighty lashes is imposed on him because he committed qadhf.
3
Determine the permissibility of remarriage
Despite the separation through li'an, the marriage can be renewed because the previous nikah is dissolved.
4
Understand the rationale
The marriage ended with li'an's separation, so a new marriage is possible, and the hadd cleanses the sin of false accusation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن أكذب نفسه حد، وحل له نكاحها
قوله : فإن ; أي بعدما لا عن قال : كذبت عليها فيما رميتها به، أقيم عليه حد القذف، وحل له أن ينكحها لزوال النكاح السابق بالتفريق بعد التلاعن ; لعدم بقاء
If he admits himself to be false, he is flogged, and her marriage to him becomes permissible. He said: 'if' i.e., after he performed li'an, he said: I lied against her in what I accused her of, so the punishment for false accusation (hadd al-qadhf) is established against him, and it becomes permissible for him to marry her because the previous marriage has ended by the separation after li'an, because there remains no...
Source pages p. 437

Commentary
This passage covers the case where the husband, after having gone through li'an, admits that he was lying in his accusation. In such a case, he is subject to the hadd punishment for false accusation (qadhf), which is eighty lashes. Additionally, despite the separation that occurred through li'an, it becomes permissible for him to remarry his ex-wife, since the marriage has ended. The commentator reasons that the marriage ended by the separation after li'an, so there is no impediment to a new marriage. The text cuts off but the ruling is clear: the hadd is mandatory and remarriage is halal.

Key Terms
حد القذف (hadd al-qadhf): The fixed punishment of eighty lashes for falsely accusing a chaste person of adultery.
أكذب نفسه (akdhaba nafsahu): The husband admits that his accusation was false after having performed li'an.

Li'an Separation Timing: Hanafi vs. Shafi'i
States judge's actions after li'an and dispute with Shafi'i on timing of separation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school holds that separation after li'an occurs only after both spouses complete li'an and evaluate the Shafi'i claim that it occurs after the husband's li'an alone.

Core Idea
Separation in li'an requires both spouses' oaths; the judge must pronounce it – the marriage continues until then.

In this passage, the commentator expands on the judge's role after li'an and clarifies a critical dispute with Imam al-Shafi'i. While both schools agree that li'an results in separation, they differ on when exactly that separation takes place. The Hanafi position, grounded in the Prophet's ﷺ practice, holds that the marriage remains intact until both spouses have completed the mutual imprecation and the judge formally pronounces the divorce. Understanding this difference is essential for determining inheritance rights and the legal status of the wife during the li'an process.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Begin by stating clearly what the Hanafi school holds: separation after li'an occurs only after both spouses have completed their oaths and the judge pronounces the separation.
The passage states that the judge 'then separates' after the entire li'an process.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Identify Imam al-Shafi'i and his followers as the opponents. They hold that separation occurs immediately upon the husband's li'an, before the wife's li'an.
The text says: 'Al-Shafi'i and those who agree with him held that separation occurs by the husband’s li'an before her li'an.'
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Although the text does not detail the Shafi'i proof, note that their reasoning likely relies on the idea that the husband's oath itself creates a dissolution. In this step, infer the basis from the context.
A common Shafi'i argument is that the husband's li'an serves as a form of divorce.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
Examine how the Hanafi school refutes the Shafi'i view. The passage cites the Prophet's ﷺ silence when asked to pronounce divorce immediately after the husband's li'an. This silence indicates that the marriage was still valid and subject to ordinary divorce.
The Prophet said, 'She is not your wife until you divorce her,' implying the marriage continued.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Identify the practical consequence of this difference. For example, if the husband dies after his li'an but before the wife's li'an, according to Shafi'i she is already divorced and does not inherit; according to Hanafi she is still a wife and inherits.
This is the key practical outcome of the dispute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الطلاق باب اللعان
القاضي بينهما ، وإن قَذَفَ بنفي الولد، أو به ، وبالزنا، ذكرا فيه ما قذف به ، ثُمَّ يفرق القاضي، وينفي نسبه، ويُلحقه بأمه، وتبين بطلقة، فإن أكذب نفسَهُ حدّ ، وحل له نكاحها
ثم يفرِّقُ القاضي ، وينفي نسبه ، ويُلحقه بأمه، وتبين بطلقة ، فإن أكذب نفسَهُ حُدّ ، وحل له نكاحها ولو كانت الفرقة حصلت بنفس التلاعن لأنكر عليه رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم في إيقاعه الطلقات، ويقال له : هي ليست زوجتك حتى : تطلقها، فسكوته دلّ على أنها محلّ لوقوع الطلاق، وإنّ الفرقة لم تحصل بعد، وأخرج الشافعي ومن وافقه في أن الفرقة تحصل بلعان الزوج قبل لعانها، وهو قول يردّه الكتاب والسنة.
[١] قوله : القاضي ; فلو لم يفرّق حتى عزل أو مات استأنف الحاكم الثاني اللعان، ثمّ يُفرِّق خلافاً لمحمد الله ، فإن قال : لا يحتاجُ إلى استئنافه. كذا في «الاختيار شرح المختار»
[٢] قوله : وينفي ; أي يصرّح الحاكم بنفي نسب الولد عنه ، ويقول : قطعت نسب هذا الولد عنه، بعدما قال : فرقت بينكما، وليس من ضرورة التفريق نفي النسب، كما إذا وقع التلاعن بعد موت الولد، فإنّه حينئذ يفرق بينهما، ولا ينتفي النسب. كذا في «النهاية».
[٣]قوله : ويلحقه ; من الإلحاق ; أي يلحق القاضي ذلك الولد بالأم على ما هو الحكم في أولاد الزنا، كذلك فعل رسول الله الله في ولد هلال بن أمية، وكان قد قذف زوجته بالزنا، ونفى الولد ) ، أخرجه أبو داود وغيره.
[٤]قوله : وتبين ; أي تصيرُ المرأةُ بعد تفريق الحاكم بائنة بطلقة، وتكون الفرقة في حكم الطلاق البائن ; لأنَّ المقصود دفع الظلم عنها ، وحصول الانقطاع التام بينهما.
[0]قوله : فإن ; أي بعدما لا عن قال : كذبت عليها فيما رميتها به، أقيم عليه حد القذف، وحلّ له أن ينكحها لزوال النكاح السابق بالتفريق بعد التلاعن ; لعدم بقاء
Book of Divorce, Chapter of Li'an. The judge [acts] between them. If he accuses by negation of lineage, or by it, and by adultery, they mention in it what he accused with. Then the judge separates, negates his lineage, and attaches him to his mother, and she becomes separated with one irrevocable divorce. If he accuses himself, he is punished for qadhf, and her marriage becomes permissible for him. Then the judge separates, negates his lineage, and attaches him to his mother, and she becomes separated with one irrevocable divorce. If he accuses himself, he is punished for qadhf, and her marriage becomes permissible for him. Even if the separation occurred by the li'an itself, the Prophet ﷺ would reject his pronouncing of divorces, and it would be said to him: She is not your wife until you divorce her. His silence indicated that she is subject to divorce, and that the separation had not occurred. Al-Shafi‘i and those who agree with him held that separation occurs by the husband’s li'an before her li'an, and this is a statement refuted by the Book and the Sunnah. [1] His saying: 'the judge' – if he does not separate until he is removed or dies, the second judge renews the li'an, then separates, contrary to Muhammad, for he said: no need to renew it. Thus in al-Ikhtiyar Sharh al-Mukhtar. [2] His saying: 'and negates' – meaning the judge explicitly negates the lineage of the child from him, and says: I cut the lineage of this child from him, after he said: I separate you two. Negation of lineage is not necessarily entailed by separation, as when li'an occurs after the child’s death; then he separates between them, but the lineage is not negated. Thus in al-Nihaya. [3] His saying: 'and attaches him' – from ilhaq; meaning the judge attaches that child to the mother according to the ruling for children of zina. Likewise the Messenger of Allah ﷺ did concerning the child of Hilal ibn Umayya, when he had accused his wife of zina and denied the child. Reported by Abu Dawud and others. [4] His saying: 'and she becomes separated' – meaning the woman after the judge’s separation becomes irreversibly divorced by one divorce, and the separation is considered a ba'in divorce because the purpose is to remove harm from her and achieve complete severance between them. [0] His saying: 'then if' – meaning after he conducted li'an, he said: I lied about her concerning what I accused her of; then the punishment for qadhf is carried out on him, and it becomes permissible for him to marry her because the previous marriage was dissolved by the separation after li'an, due to the non-existence of [the marriage?].
Source pages p. 437

Commentary
This is an inter-madhhab dispute passage from the commentary on al-Wiqayah. The base matn states the procedure after li'an: the judge separates the couple, negates the child's lineage from the father, and attaches the child to the mother. If the husband later admits he lied, he is punished for slander (qadhf) and may remarry the woman. The commentary expands on each element. It clarifies that if the original judge dies or is removed before separating, the successor must renew the li'an (contrary to Imam Muhammad's view). Negation of lineage is separate from the divorce; if the child has already died, lineage is not negated. The proof for attaching the child to the mother is from the Prophet's action in the case of Hilal ibn Umayya. The commentary then addresses a dispute with Imam al-Shafi‘i: Shafi‘i held that the separation occurs by the husband's li'an alone, before the wife's li'an. The Hanafi response argues that the Prophet’s silence when asked to pronounce divorce indicated that separation had not yet occurred, and the wife was still eligible for divorce. Thus the Hanafi position is that separation only occurs after both spouses have completed li'an. This passage also notes an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf: if the husband admits lying (takdhib nafs), he is punished for qadhf, and the marriage becomes permissible again for him, because the previous separation via li'an dissolved the marriage.

Key Terms
اللعان (al-li‘ān): Mutual imprecation between spouses when the husband accuses his wife of adultery and denies paternity, involving oaths and curses.
قذف (qadhf): False accusation of adultery or fornication, which incurs the prescribed punishment of 80 lashes.
بائنة (bā'inah): Irrevocable divorce that ends the marriage without possibility of reconciliation except through a new contract.
تفريق (tafrīq): The judge's formal pronouncement of separation between the spouses after li'an.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Immediately upon the husband's li'an
B) After the judge pronounces the separation upon completion of both spouses' li'an
C) When the husband denies paternity for the first time
D) Automatically when the wife completes her li'an

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, separation in li'an requires both spouses to complete the mutual imprecation and the judge to formally pronounce the divorce; the marriage remains intact until that point.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Eternal Prohibition After Li'an
Intra-Hanafi dispute and additional rulings on li'an prohibition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa, Muhammad, and Abu Yusuf on the eternal prohibition after li'an and apply the condition of eligibility for li'an to determine when the prohibition ends.

Core Idea
The eternal prohibition after li'an is conditional on eligibility; the hadith's 'abadan' binds only while li'an status holds.

This passage explores a key intra-Hanafi difference regarding the hadith that the two li'an participants can never reunite. While the apparent meaning suggests an absolute eternal ban, Abu Hanifa and Imam Muhammad qualify it: if the husband admits lying, the li'an is nullified and the prohibition lifts. Abu Yusuf holds the ban permanent. The commentary also discusses how other events—such as the wife's proven zina or the husband's qadhf conviction—also remove the prohibition by destroying eligibility for li'an.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed hadith
The hadith 'al-mutala‘inan la yajtami‘an abadan' is the core textual proof. Recognize its wording and transmission status.
The hadith is reported with a weak chain but is accepted by al-Hidayah.
2
Distinguish literal vs. qualified reading
Abu Yusuf takes the hadith as absolute eternal prohibition. Abu Hanifa and Muhammad restrict it to the duration of li'an status.
This is the central point of dispute.
3
Analyze the basis for qualification
The husband's self-incrimination (takdhib nafs) nullifies the li'an, removing the basis for the prohibition. The perpetuity is tied to the state of being mutala‘inan.
Once li'an is void, the spouses are no longer mutala‘inan.
4
Extend the logic to other scenarios
The same principle applies if the wife commits proven zina or the husband is punished for qadhf. Both destroy eligibility for li'an, ending the prohibition.
Eligibility for li'an requires chastity and being free from qadhf punishment.

When the li'an is nullified, they are no longer li'an participants, because it [the continuous prohibition] is the cause of their non-association, and the ruling is non-association. (And likewise if he accuses someone else and is punished for qadhf, the effect of li'an remains between them. [1] His statement: 'his statement...' etc. The author of al-Hidayah mentioned this hadith as marfu‘, but al-Itqani in Ghayat al-Bayan and al-‘Ayni in al-Binayah said it is not reported as marfu‘; rather it is reported from a group of Companions. Both have erred, for it is also reported as marfu‘ in Sunan al-Bayhaqi and al-Daraqutni but with a weak chain. [2] His statement: 'as long as they are...' etc. The summary is that the apparent meaning of the two hadiths necessitates that marriage is never permissible between them, as Abu Yusuf held. However, Abu Hanifa and Muhammad ruled that marriage becomes permissible after he accuses himself, based on the understanding that the perpetuity in the hadith is tied to the duration of the li'an status. [3] His statement: 'when it is nullified' — i.e., by his self-incrimination, then the punishment of qadhf becomes obligatory on him, and the eligibility for li'an is nullified. [4] His statement: 'and likewise...' etc. The summary is that if the husband after li'an accuses someone else and is punished for qadhf, or if the wife commits zina, then it becomes permissible for him to marry her, because by her zina her chastity is nullified, and by his qadhf alone his eligibility for testimony is nullified. And among the conditions for the continued permanent prohibition is the existence of eligibility for li'an in both. When that is negated, the prohibition is negated.
Source pages p. 438

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the hadith 'The two who engage in li'an shall never be together.' Abu Yusuf understood this hadith literally to mean that the spouses are perpetually forbidden to remarry each other after li'an, regardless of circumstances. Abu Hanifa and Imam Muhammad, however, interpret the hadith differently: the eternal prohibition applies only as long as the li'an status persists. Once the husband admits he lied (takdhib nafs), the li'an is nullified, the punishment for qadhf is imposed, and the prohibition ceases — the spouses may remarry. The commentary also clarifies that the hadith, though mentioned by al-Hidayah as marfu‘ (attributed to the Prophet), has a weak chain, but it is also reported from Companions. The passage then addresses two more scenarios: if after li'an the husband accuses someone else of zina and is punished for qadhf, or if the wife commits zina and is punished, the prohibition against remarriage is also lifted. The rationale: the eternal prohibition is conditioned on both spouses retaining the capacity for li'an (i.e., being free from qadhf punishment and remaining chaste). When the husband is punished for qadhf, he loses his eligibility for testimony and thus for li'an; similarly, if the wife commits proven zina, her chastity is nullified, and she too loses eligibility. Hence the prohibition ends.

Key Terms
متلاعنان (mutalā'īnān): The two spouses who have engaged in the process of li'an (mutual imprecation).
تكذيب النفس (takdhīb al-nafs): The husband's admission that he lied in his accusation during li'an.
أهلية اللعان (ahliyyat al-li‘ān): Eligibility to participate in li'an, which requires being free from punishment for qadhf and being chaste.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the mufta bihi Hanafi position, what is the effect of the husband admitting he lied (takdhib nafs) after completing li'an?
A) The spouses remain forever forbidden to remarry.
B) The marriage becomes permissible for him to remarry her.
C) The husband is punished for qadhf but the prohibition remains.
D) The li'an must be repeated.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) holds that the prohibition after li'an is not eternal; it ends when the husband admits lying or when either spouse loses eligibility for li'an.

Termination of Li'an's Effect and Conditions for Validity
States rulings on when li'an's permanent prohibition ends and when li'an cannot be performed.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which li'an's eternal prohibition ends and identify scenarios where li'an is not valid.

Core Idea
Li'an's prohibition ends when capacity for li'an is lost; li'an is not valid from a dumb person or for denying pregnancy.

The passage from 'Umda al-Ri'aya expands on the ruling that li'an imposes an eternal prohibition on marriage between the spouses. However, this eternity is not absolute; it is conditioned on the continuation of the capacity for li'an. If either party loses that capacity—through the husband's false accusation leading to hadd punishment, or the wife's zina leading to hadd—the prohibition lifts. Additionally, li'an itself is not valid from a speechless person or for denying a mere pregnancy, because li'an substitutes for the hadd of qadhf and requires certainty. These rulings illustrate the Hanafi school's careful balance between textual literalism and contextual reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule
The hadith says 'the two who perform li'an never come together forever'. Understand that this establishes an eternal prohibition of marriage after li'an.
This is the starting point for the discussion.
2
Identify the exceptions
The passage lists cases where the prohibition ends: if the husband after li'an accuses someone else and is punished for qadhf, or if the wife commits zina and is punished. In both, the capacity for li'an is lost.
Also, retraction by the husband (not explicitly mentioned but implied) ends the prohibition.
3
Understand the underlying condition
The key concept is that the eternal prohibition is conditional on the continuation of the 'ahliyya' (capacity) for li'an in both spouses. When this capacity is lost, the prohibition ceases.
This resolves the apparent conflict between the hadith and the exceptions.
4
Recognize when li'an is not valid initially
Li'an is not established by the accusation of a dumb person (who cannot speak) because li'an replaces the hadd of qadhf, which requires explicit verbalization. It is also not valid for denying a pregnancy (as opposed to a live child) because pregnancy is uncertain.
Uncertainty (shubha) averts the harsh consequences of li'an.
5
Note the intra-Hanafi dispute
Abu Yusuf held the prohibition is absolute based on the hadith's apparent meaning, while Abu Hanifa and Muhammad conditioned it on the continuation of li'an. Also on pregnancy denial, Abu Hanifa and Zufar say no li'an, while Sahibayn require it if she gives birth at <6 months.
The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is Abu Hanifa's view.

[1] His statement: 'she is punished' i.e., the hadd punishment for zina is carried out on her, as in Al-Hidayah and others. It was objected that her punishment is stoning, so her becoming permissible for the husband is inconceivable; rather, merely by her committing zina she exits from capacity. As in Al-Fath. (2) His statement: 'the dumb person' i.e., one who cannot speak; because li'an stands in place of the hadd punishment for qadhf, so it is attached to explicit verbalization, and is averted by doubt. [3] His statement: 'denial of pregnancy' in the genitive, coordinated with 'accusation of the dumb person', meaning when he does not accuse her of zina and does not deny her existing live child, but denies the pregnancy, by saying explicitly: 'your pregnancy is not from me', then li'an is not obligatory due to it; because it is not certain that she is pregnant, even if signs appear; since it could be a swelling or wind, so his denial of pregnancy is not qadhf against her, whether she gives birth afterwards at less than six months or more.
Source pages p. 438

Commentary
This passage from 'Umda al-Ri'aya is a commentary on the matn of Wiqaya discussing exceptions to the permanent prohibition of li'an and conditions for its validity. The commentary first explains that if after li'an the husband commits qadhf of another person and is punished, or the wife commits zina and is punished, the eternal prohibition of marriage between them ends. This is because the capacity (ahliyya) for li'an is lost: the husband's eligibility for testimony is invalidated by his false accusation, and the wife's chastity is lost by her zina. The commentator notes an intra-madhhab dispute: Abu Yusuf held that the prohibition is eternal based on the apparent meaning of hadith, while Abu Hanifa and Muhammad hold it ceases once the husband retracts his accusation, since the hadith's 'eternal' is conditioned on the continuation of li'an. The hadith itself is discussed: despite claims it is not marfu', it is reported marfu' with a weak chain. Secondly, the passage states that li'an is not established by the accusation of a dumb person (who cannot speak) because li'an substitutes for the hadd of qadhf, which requires explicit speech, and is averted by doubt. Thirdly, li'an is not valid for denying a mere pregnancy (as opposed to a live child) because pregnancy is uncertain; it may be a swelling. This is Abu Hanifa and Zufar's view. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold that if she gives birth at less than six months, li'an is required, as that confirms zina. The commentary also notes a problem in Al-Hidayah's phrasing: if the wife is punished, her punishment for zina is stoning, which would kill her, so the husband remarrying her is not applicable; rather, merely her zina itself removes capacity. The passage carefully distinguishes between cases that remove the effect of li'an and cases where li'an is not initially valid.

Key Terms
اللعان (al-li‘ān): A mutual imprecation between spouses when the husband accuses his wife of zina but lacks witnesses.
الأخرس (al-akhras): A person who cannot speak; his qadhf does not trigger li'an because li'an requires explicit verbalization.
نفي الحمل (nafy al-haml): Denial of the wife's pregnancy (as opposed to denying a live child).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Li'an's prohibition is not eternal if the capacity for li'an is lost, and li'an is not valid from a dumb person or for denying pregnancy.

Li'an for the Mute Husband
Ruling on li'an for a mute husband.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on li'an when the husband is mute and explain why li'an is not valid based on the requirement of explicit speech.

Core Idea
Li'an requires explicit verbal utterance; a mute cannot perform it.

In this lesson, we examine the case of a mute husband who accuses his wife of zina. The Hanafi school holds that li'an is not valid because it substitutes for the hadd penalty for false accusation (qadhf), which demands clear speech. This ruling illustrates a key principle: hudud are repelled by doubts, and muteness introduces a doubt that prevents the full li'an procedure.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The act is li'an initiated by a mute husband. The ruling is that it is not valid (la yajib).
This is the position of the Hanafi school.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is that li'an stands in place of hadd al-qadhf, which requires explicit verbal utterance (sarih al-nutq). Since a mute cannot speak, li'an cannot be performed.
The commentary states: 'li'an substitutes for hadd al-qadhf, so it is connected to clear speech.'
3
Apply the principle of shubuhat
Hudud are repelled by doubts (shubuhat). Muteness creates a doubt about the ability to formulate the required oath, so li'an is prevented.
The phrase 'yundari' bi al-shubha' appears in the commentary.
4
Determine the practical consequence
Since li'an is not valid, the mute husband may be subject to hadd al-qadhf if he accuses his wife via writing or gestures, because those are not sufficient for li'an but could constitute qadhf.
The text implies that the accusation remains effective for hadd but not for li'an.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Or if she committed zina and was punished with hadd, and no li'an for the accusation of a mute, and for denial of pregnancy from him, even if she gives birth in less than six months, or if she committed zina and was punished with hadd (1) (1): meaning it is permissible for him to marry her if he accused someone else after li'an and was punished with hadd, or she committed zina after li'an and was punished with hadd, because the continuity of the eligibility for li'an is a condition for the continuity of its ruling. Commentary
This passage discusses two conditions under which li'an is not valid in the Hanafi school. First, li'an is not valid if the husband is mute (akhras) because li'an substitutes for the hadd penalty for false accusation (qadhf), which requires explicit verbal utterance (sarih al-nutq). Since a mute cannot speak clearly, the li'an cannot be performed; instead, the husband may be subject to hadd al-qadhf if he writes or gestures. This follows the principle that li'an, like hudud, is repelled by doubts (shubuhat). Second, li'an is not valid for denial of pregnancy (nafi al-haml) as opposed to denial of an existing child. Denying a pregnancy is not a definitive accusation of zina because the pregnancy may not be real—it could be wind or water retention. Abu Hanifa and Zufar hold that even if the wife subsequently gives birth in less than six months, li'an is still not required because at the time of denial, the pregnancy was not certain; the denial becomes a conditional statement ('if you are pregnant, it is not from me'), and conditional accusations (ta'liq) are not valid for qadhf. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad disagree: if she gives birth in less than six months, it is proven that the pregnancy existed at the time of denial, making it a direct accusation, so li'an is required. The commentary by the author of 'Umdat al-Ri'aya explains this reasoning and cites Fath al-Qadir for the illustrative story of a woman who experienced a false pregnancy.

Key Terms
اللعان (al-li'an): A mutual oath-taking process between spouses when the husband accuses his wife of zina or denies paternity of a child; it substitutes for the hadd penalty for qadhf.
الأخرس (al-akhras): A mute person who cannot speak; in the context of li'an, one who cannot utter the required verbal declarations.
القذف (al-qadhf): Accusation of zina; a legal offense that entails a hadd penalty unless proven.
الشبهة (al-shubha): Doubt; in legal context, any uncertainty that repels the application of hadd penalties.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, why is li'an not valid for a mute husband?
A) Because the mute is not legally responsible for his actions.
B) Because li'an substitutes for hadd al-qadhf, which requires explicit verbal utterance.
C) Because the wife cannot defend herself against a mute's accusation.
D) Because li'an can only be performed in Arabic, and the mute cannot speak Arabic.

Key Takeaway
Li'an by a mute husband is invalid because it substitutes for a hadd that requires explicit speech, and muteness is a doubt that repels the procedure.

Denial of Pregnancy and the Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on denial of pregnancy as a cause for li'an.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why denial of pregnancy does not trigger li'an according to Abu Hanifa and contrast the position of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad.

Core Idea
Denial of pregnancy is not a definitive accusation; conditional statements do not trigger li'an.

This lesson examines the case where a husband denies his wife's pregnancy rather than an existing child. The Hanafi school is divided: Abu Hanifa and Zufar hold that li'an is not required even if she later gives birth in less than six months, while Abu Yusuf and Muhammad argue that li'an becomes wajib in that situation. The dispute hinges on whether the denial is considered a conditional accusation (ta'liq), which is invalid for hudud, or a definitive accusation once the pregnancy is confirmed by birth within the minimal gestation period.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act being considered
The husband denies the pregnancy itself, not a living child. He says 'Your pregnancy is not from me'.
This is different from denying paternity of an existing child.
2
State Abu Hanifa's ruling
Li'an is not required (la yajib) even if the wife gives birth in less than six months.
This is the position of Abu Hanifa and Zufar.
3
Analyze the dalil for Abu Hanifa
The denial is conditional on the existence of pregnancy. At the time of denial, the pregnancy is not certain, so it is a conditional accusation (ta'liq), and conditional qadhf is not valid. Additionally, li'an falls under hudud, which are repelled by doubts; the possibility that the pregnancy is not real (e.g., wind) is a doubt.
The text mentions 'it is possible that it is inflation or wind'.
4
State the position of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad
If the wife gives birth in less than six months, li'an is required (wajib) because the pregnancy is proven to have existed at the time of denial, making it a direct accusation.
The opposing view: the birth within six months retroactively confirms the pregnancy.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Under Abu Hanifa, the child is attributed to the husband and no li'an is needed. Under the Sahibayn, li'an must be performed, and the child is disowned if li'an is completed.
This is the practical consequence of the dispute.

Commentary
This passage discusses two conditions under which li'an is not valid in the Hanafi school. First, li'an is not valid if the husband is mute (akhras) because li'an substitutes for the hadd penalty for false accusation (qadhf), which requires explicit verbal utterance (sarih al-nutq). Since a mute cannot speak clearly, the li'an cannot be performed; instead, the husband may be subject to hadd al-qadhf if he writes or gestures. This follows the principle that li'an, like hudud, is repelled by doubts (shubuhat). Second, li'an is not valid for denial of pregnancy (nafi al-haml) as opposed to denial of an existing child. Denying a pregnancy is not a definitive accusation of zina because the pregnancy may not be real—it could be wind or water retention. Abu Hanifa and Zufar hold that even if the wife subsequently gives birth in less than six months, li'an is still not required because at the time of denial, the pregnancy was not certain; the denial becomes a conditional statement ('if you are pregnant, it is not from me'), and conditional accusations (ta'liq) are not valid for qadhf. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad disagree: if she gives birth in less than six months, it is proven that the pregnancy existed at the time of denial, making it a direct accusation, so li'an is required. The commentary by the author of 'Umdat al-Ri'aya explains this reasoning and cites Fath al-Qadir for the illustrative story of a woman who experienced a false pregnancy.

Key Terms
نفي الحمل (nafi al-haml): Denial of pregnancy; the husband's statement that his wife's pregnancy is not from him, as opposed to denying an existing child.
التعليق (al-ta'liq): A conditional statement; here, the husband says 'if you are pregnant, it is not from me', which is a conditional denial not actionable for li'an.
القذف (al-qadhf): Accusation of zina; a legal offense that entails a hadd penalty unless proven.

The Invalidity of Conditional Qadhf in Li'an
Ruling on li'an when pregnancy existence is not certain

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a conditional denial of paternity is invalid in Hanafi law and apply the ruling that qadhf cannot be conditional to scenarios of uncertain pregnancy.

Core Idea
Qadhf cannot be conditional; an uncertain pregnancy does not allow negation of lineage.

In Hanafi fiqh, li'an is a solemn procedure to resolve accusations of zina without witnesses, but its validity depends on certainty. When a husband says, 'If you are pregnant, it is not from me,' he attempts to attach the accusation to a future event. The passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah explains that such a conditional statement is invalid because qadhf (slander) – being a hadd punishment – cannot be made conditional. This lesson unpacks the reasoning, the intra-Hanafi nuance on pregnancy certainty, and the practical implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Husband's Statement and the Ruling
The husband says, 'You committed zina and this pregnancy is from that' or 'If you are pregnant, it is not from me.' The ruling is that the couple performs li'an, but the judge does not immediately negate the lineage of the pregnancy.
The pregnancy's existence at the time of denial is not certain.
2
Understand the Invalidity of Conditional Qadhf
If the husband's denial is conditional on a future event (e.g., birth before six months), it becomes a conditional qadhf. Hanafi law holds that qadhf cannot be attached to a condition (ta'liq) because it is a hadd punishment.
Hudud require immediate and definite proof; conditions introduce uncertainty.
3
Analyze the Intra-Hanafi Difference on Certainty
Abu Hanifa and Zufar hold that pregnancy is not certain at the time of denial; it could be wind or water. The text illustrates this with a story from Fath al-Qadir of a false pregnancy. Thus, even if the birth occurs later, the original denial was conditional and invalid.
Other Hanafis may argue that birth before six months retroactively confirms pregnancy, but the principle of no conditional qadhf prevails.
4
Distinguish Li'an from Other Rulings
The commentary contrasts li'an with inheritance, bequest, manumission (which can be conditional), and returning a sold slave girl due to pregnancy (which is allowed despite doubt). Li'an is from the category of hudud, so it cannot be treated like defect claims.
Lineage is established by doubt (shubhah) in other contexts, but hudud demand certainty.
5
Apply the Ruling to a Practical Scenario
If a husband says, 'If you are pregnant, it is not from me,' and she gives birth after a full term, the paternity is still attributed to him because the denial was conditional and invalid. He must either perform proper li'an (with immediate, unconditional accusation) or be liable for hadd qadhf.
The judge never negates the pregnancy; the child's lineage remains intact.

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the chapter of li'an. It addresses the situation where a husband accuses his wife of zina and denies paternity of her pregnancy, but the pregnancy's existence is uncertain at the time of his denial. The matn states that the couple performs li'an, but the judge does not immediately negate the lineage of the pregnancy. The commentator explains that if she gives birth before six months (the minimum pregnancy duration), it becomes clear that the pregnancy existed at the time of denial. However, Abu Hanifa and Zufar hold that the existence of pregnancy is not certain at the time of denial because it could be wind or water. The commentary from Fath al-Qadir illustrates this with a story of a woman whose 'pregnancy' turned out to be water. If she gives birth before six months, the denial becomes conditional ('If you are pregnant, it is not from me'), but qadhf (slander) cannot be made conditional. Therefore, such a conditional denial is invalid and not considered. The commentary then distinguishes li'an from other rulings: inheritance and bequest are established only after birth for the child, not for the fetus; manumission can be conditional; returning a sold slave girl due to pregnancy is allowed despite doubt because it is a defect claim, but li'an is prevented because it is a hadd (fixed punishment) and lineage is established by doubt. Thus, li'an cannot be based on uncertain conditions. The key principle: li'an is a form of hadd, so it cannot be dependent on a future event.

Key Terms
تلاعن (tala'ana): The mutual imprecation (li'an) between husband and wife, where each invokes curses upon themselves if lying.
قذف (qadhf): False accusation of adultery, which incurs a fixed punishment (hadd) of eighty lashes.
تعليق (ta'liq): Conditional attachment of a ruling or act to a future event.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) Because the wife may not be pregnant

Key Takeaway
A conditional denial of paternity is invalid because qadhf cannot be conditional; uncertain pregnancy does not trigger lineage negation.

Validity of Denying Paternity: The Period of Congratulations
Ruling on the validity of denial of paternity during the period of congratulations and the purchase of baby supplies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which denial of paternity is valid in Hanafi law and explain the intra-madhhab ikhtilaf on the duration of the tahni'ah period.

Core Idea
Denial valid only during the customary period of congratulations or purchase of baby supplies; silence implies acknowledgment.

This passage from the chapter of li'an addresses when a husband can deny paternity of a newborn child. The Hanafi school holds that if the husband speaks up during the period when congratulations are offered or when baby supplies are purchased, his denial is effective. If he remains silent during this time, his silence is considered an implicit acknowledgment, and subsequent denial is invalid. The exact duration of the 'period of congratulations' is subject to dispute within the school, with the preferred view leaving it to custom.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is denying paternity of a newborn. The ruling is that it is valid if done during the time of tahni'ah or at the purchase of baby supplies; after that it is invalid.
2
Understand the effect of silence
If the husband accepts congratulations or remains silent during that period, it is considered an implicit acknowledgment that prevents later denial.
3
Examine the dispute on the duration of tahni'ah
Some Hanafi narrations say 3 days, others 7; Abu Yusuf and Muhammad say the period of nifas; the most correct view is no fixed limit, left to custom.
4
Note the practical consequence after the period
After the period, denial does not negate lineage but still triggers li'an because it is a qadhf accusation.
5
Apply the principle to a scenario
Consider: if a husband receives congratulations and remains silent, then denies after a week, his denial is invalid for lineage but he must still perform li'an.

(۱) ولم يعين لها مقداراً في ظاهر الرواية، وذكر أبو الليث عن أبي حنيفة له تقديرها بثلاثة أيام، وروى الحسن عنه : سبعة ؛ لأنها أيام التهنئة. وضعفه السرخسي بأن نصب المقادير بالرأي متعذر. مقدرة بمدة النفاس ؛ لأنها أثر الولادة. ينظر : «فتح القدير»(٤ : ٢٩٥). (٢) أي ولدين من بطن واحد بين ولادتهما أقل من ستة أشهر. ينظر : «مجمع الأنهر»(١ : ٤٦٠).
Book of Divorce, Chapter of Li'an. 221. And whoever denies the child at the time of congratulations, or at the purchase of delivery equipment, his denial is valid, and after that it is not valid. And he must perform li'an in both cases. And if he denies the first of twins and acknowledges the other, he incurs the hadd punishment because he contradicted himself by claiming the second; because they were created from one water. His statement: 'Your pregnancy is not from me' is pointless in the chapter of li'an, and the judge does not negate the pregnancy at that time because there is no ruling on it before its birth. [1] His statement: 'time of congratulations' – with a hamza – from 'I congratulated him on the child' in Persian: mubarakbadi dadan. And this time is estimated as three days, and in one narration: seven days. And according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), it is the duration of nifas, and the most correct is that there is no estimation, rather it is referred to custom. [2] His statement: 'delivery equipment' – meaning the goods and tools needed at birth, like the cradle, swaddling, wrappings, the item in which the child is placed at birth, and things in which the child is wrapped. [3] His statement: 'after that it is not valid' – its summary: If he denies the child and says 'He is not from me' at the time of congratulations or at the purchase of delivery equipment, his denial is valid; after that it is not valid, because if he accepted congratulations or remained silent at that time or at the purchase of delivery equipment, that becomes an implicit acknowledgment from him, so his denial after that is not valid. [4] His statement: 'in both cases' – meaning, whether he denies the child at the time of congratulations or after it, he must perform li'an due to the existence of qadhf. Thus, li'an is confirmed by denial of the child, but the lineage is not negated in the second scenario. [0] His statement: 'twins' – they are the two children whose births are separated by less than six months. Its summary: If she gives birth to twins, and he denies the first and says 'He is not from me' and acknowledges the other, whether he did not... (1) And it was not specified with a measure in the apparent narration. Abu al-Layth reported from Abu Hanifa that he estimated it as three days, and al-Hasan narrated from him: seven days because they are the days of congratulations. al-Sarakhsi weakened this because establishing measures by opinion is difficult. According to the two, it is estimated by the duration of nifas because it is an effect of birth. See: Fath al-Qadir (4:295). (2) That is, two children from one womb with less than six months between their births. See: Majma' al-Anhur (1:460).
Source pages p. 441

Commentary
This passage provides two rulings from the chapter of li'an. The first ruling deals with the timing of denying paternity. If the husband denies the child during the period of congratulations (tahni'ah) or when baby supplies are bought, his denial is valid. However, if he remains silent during these occasions, it is considered an implicit acknowledgment of paternity, and his subsequent denial is invalid. In both cases, he must perform li'an because the denial itself constitutes qadhf. The duration of the tahni'ah period is disputed within the Hanafi school: some narrations from Abu Hanifa say three days, others seven, and according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad it is the period of nifas (postpartum bleeding). The most correct position (al-sahih) is that there is no fixed limit; it is left to custom (urf). The second ruling addresses the denial of twins: if the wife gives birth to twins (with intervals less than six months) and the husband denies the first but acknowledges the second, he incurs the hadd punishment for qadhf because he contradicts himself. Both twins come from the same water (semen), so acknowledging one implies acknowledgment of the other. This shows that denial must be consistent.

Key Terms
تهنئة (tahni'ah): The period of congratulations given to the father upon the birth of a child, typically within a few days.
آلة الولادة (alat al-wiladah): Delivery equipment; the goods and tools needed for a newborn, such as a cradle, swaddling, etc.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the preferred Hanafi view, how is the duration of the tahni'ah period determined?
A) Three days, as narrated from Abu Hanifa.
B) Seven days, as narrated from al-Hasan.
C) The period of nifas (postpartum bleeding).
D) It is left to custom (urf); no fixed limit.

Key Takeaway
Denial of paternity must be made promptly; silence at the time of congratulations or purchase of supplies is an implicit acknowledgment that prevents later denial.

Denial of One Twin: Contradiction and Hadd
Ruling on the denial of one twin and acknowledgment of the other.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why denying one twin while acknowledging the other leads to hadd punishment in Hanafi law.

Core Idea
Twins from one womb are from one water; denying one while acknowledging the other is a contradictory claim that incurs the legal penalty.

This passage addresses a specific scenario: a wife gives birth to twins, and the husband denies paternity of the first twin but acknowledges the second. The Hanafi school rules that this contradiction is impossible because both twins originate from the same act of conception. Therefore, the husband's denial is treated as a false accusation (qadhf), and he must face the hadd punishment.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
Twins are born with an interval of less than six months between births, indicating they are from one pregnancy.
2
Identify the husband's inconsistent claim
He denies the first twin but acknowledges the second as his child.
3
Understand the legal reasoning
Both twins are created from one water (semen); therefore, they share the same father. Acknowledging one implies the other is also his child.
4
Determine the consequence
Since his denial of the first twin is a false accusation, he incurs the hadd punishment for qadhf.
5
Note the exception
If the husband had denied both twins, there would be no contradiction; he would need to perform li'an for each.

Timing of Paternity Denial and Li'an Obligation
Ruling on paternity denial timing and li'an

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the timing of paternity denial, explain why silence at congratulation constitutes tacit acknowledgment, and identify the relied-upon view on the duration of the congratulation period.

Core Idea
Silence at congratulation equals acknowledgment; denial after that is void, but li'an is still required.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya clarifies a critical rule in li'an: a husband's paternity denial is only valid during the customary window when congratulations are offered or birth supplies purchased. His silence or acceptance during this period is legally considered an acknowledgment (iqrar dilala), so later denial is ineffective. However, even if denial is delayed, he must still perform li'an to avoid the penalty for false accusation. The commentary also explores an intra-Hanafi difference on the exact duration of this window, settling on the view that it is determined by custom.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its legal effect
The ruling is about a husband denying paternity. If done during the time of congratulation or purchase of birth supplies, the denial is valid and lineage is negated after li'an. If done after that period, the denial is invalid and lineage remains.
2
Understand tacit acknowledgment (iqrar dilala)
The text explains that accepting congratulations or remaining silent at that time constitutes an implied admission of paternity. Legally, this binds the husband so that he cannot later deny.
3
Examine the two scenarios
First, if he denies at the proper time: valid, and li'an is required to confirm the denial. Second, if he denies later: denial is invalid, but li'an is still required to avoid the hadd of qadhf, though lineage is not affected.
4
Analyze the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on duration
The commentators report that some narrations from Abu Hanifa fix the time as three or seven days; Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani equate it with nifas. The most sound view is that no fixed limit is set; it is determined by custom.
5
Apply the mufta bihi position
In practice, the relied-upon view leaves the duration to customary practice. A mufti would ask what is the local custom for offering congratulations after childbirth and rule accordingly.

(۱) ولم يعين لها مقداراً في ظاهر الرواية، وذكر أبو الليث عن أبي حنيفة له تقديرها بثلاثة أيام، وروى الحسن عنه : سبعة ؛ لأنها أيام التهنئة. وضعفه السرخسي بأن نصب المقادير بالرأي متعذر. مقدرة بمدة النفاس ؛ لأنها أثر الولادة. ينظر : «فتح القدير»(٤ : ٢٩٥).
And whoever denies the child at the time of congratulation or purchase of birth supplies, his denial is valid, but after that it is not, and he must perform li'an in both cases. (And whoever denies the child at the time of congratulation or purchase of birth supplies, his denial is valid, but after that it is not, and he must perform li'an in both cases): that is, in the case of denial at the time of congratulation and in the case of denial after the time of congratulation. He said: Your pregnancy is not from me is idle talk in the chapter of li'an, and the judge does not rule on the pregnancy then because there is no ruling before its birth. [1] He said: the time of congratulation – with hamza – from 'I congratulated him on the child' in Persian: mubarakbadi dadan, and this time is estimated as three days, and in a narration: seven days. According to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), it is the duration of postpartum bleeding (nifas), and the soundest view is that there is no fixed estimate, rather it is referred to custom. [2] He said: birth supplies – that is, the utensils and tools needed at birth, like the cradle, swaddling, wrapping, the thing in which the child is placed at birth, and the things in which the child is wrapped. [3] He said: after it, no – the gist is that if he denies the child and says 'he is not from me' at the time of congratulation or purchase of birth supplies, his denial is valid, but not after that, because when he accepted the congratulation or remained silent at it or at the purchase of birth supplies, that becomes a tacit acknowledgment of the child, so his denial after that is not valid. [4] He said: in both cases – that is, whether he denies the child at the time of congratulation or after it, he must perform li'an because of the accusation (qadhf), so li'an is established by denial of the child, but lineage is not negated in the second case. Source pages p. 441

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with commentary from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya on the chapter of li'an. It states the legal rule concerning a husband's denial of paternity in relation to the customary period of congratulation and purchase of birth supplies. The ruling: if the husband denies the child during the time when people congratulate him or when birth supplies are bought, his denial is effective in law. However, if he remains silent at that time and denies later, his denial is invalid because his silence is considered a tacit acknowledgment. In both cases, he must still perform li'an because he has accused his wife (qadhf) by denying the child, but the lineage is only negated if the denial was timely. The commentary explains key terms: 'zaman al-tahanina' (time of congratulation) is customarily estimated as three or seven days according to different narrations from Abu Hanifa, while Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani hold it equals the period of nifas (postpartum bleeding). The most correct view is that no fixed time is set; it is left to custom. 'Alat al-wilada' (birth supplies) refers to items like the cradle and swaddling. The legal reasoning is based on tacit acknowledgment (iqrar dilala): accepting congratulation or purchasing supplies implicitly affirms the child, so later denial is contradictory and ineffective. The passage also clarifies that even if denial is invalid, li'an is still required to avoid the hadd of qadhf, though the lineage remains established.

Key Terms
زمان التهنئة (zaman al-tahanina): The time when people congratulate the father on the birth of a child; customarily estimated at three or seven days, or the period of postpartum bleeding (nifas), but the soundest view is that it is determined by custom.
آلة الولادة (alat al-wilada): Utensils and tools needed at childbirth, such as the cradle, swaddling, wrappings, etc.
الإقرار دلالة (al-iqrar dilala): Tacit acknowledgment inferred from conduct, such as accepting congratulations or buying birth supplies, which implies acceptance of paternity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is a husband's denial of paternity invalid after the time of congratulation?
A) Because the husband missed a fixed three-day deadline set by the Prophet (ﷺ)
B) Because his silence or acceptance at the time of congratulation amounts to tacit acknowledgment of the child
C) Because he must perform li'an before denying
D) Because the child is already born and physical paternity is proven

Key Takeaway
A husband's silent acceptance of congratulations is legally binding as acknowledgment; delayed paternity denial is invalid, but li'an is still obligatory.
